==PAGE:2==
Preface
This thesis is written by Therese Rahbek Wassberg (Graduate student, Biomedical engineer-
ing at Technical University of Denmark (DTU)) and Gitte Bailey Hass (Graduate student,
Biomedical engineering at Technical University of Denmark (DTU)) as a part of the work
done to investigate embedded 3D bioprinting of skeletal muscle tissue models and optimizing
protocols for printing muscle tissue precursors into support gels.
This thesis introduces the principles of extrusion based 3D printing and embedded 3D bio-
printing for creating a 3D model of skeletal muscle to a broad audience ranging from non-
engineering students to graduate level students in engineering and science. The model is
based on mouse myoblast cells (C2C12), therefore the thesis includes principles behind ap-
plying in vitro cell culture as basis for creating bioinks for 3D embedded bioprinting of
skeletal muscle precursors.
This thesis contains theory sections for skeletal muscle tissue, how to do 3D printing and em-
bedded 3D bioprinting, how to work with C2C12 cells and how to do microscopy for cells and
bioink evaluation. Additionally the materials and methods needed to perform experiments
are described. The results are presented and discussed later in the thesis. The information
is obtained from reading suitable articles and literature. During data processing, ImageJ
and ZEISS ZEN (blue edition) were used to obtain the desired images for visualization of
cell survival, diﬀerentiation, and muscle tissue formation.
It was only possible to make this thesis with the help of Johan Ulrik Lind (Group leader,
Associate Professor, Department of Health Technology at DTU) and Asli Aybike Doğan
(Postdoc, Department of Skeletal Muscle and Pancreas Biology at Novo Nordisk) so we
thank them for their help and guidance during our investigations. Lastly, we would like to
thank Jonathan Bendix Fjendsbo for a close collaboration during our mutual work on the
engineerd device and the rest of the TMAT (Tailored Materials and Tissues) group for their
open mindedness and help throughout the laboratory work.
i
==PAGE:3==
1
Abstract
In recent years, advanced research on three-dimensional (3D) muscle models has signiﬁcantly
contributed to understanding muscle pathology and developing more eﬀective therapeutic
strategies. These tissue models use cell-containing hydrogels cast in specially manufactured
molds. Any design changes to the tissue model require prior fabrication of modiﬁed molds.
Instead, we propose 3D printing as an alternative fabrication method that can rapidly and
cost eﬀectively increase design freedom by fabricating complex structures. This thesis demon-
strates how to optimize an embedded printing protocol for printing muscle tissue precursors,
as an alternative to tissue casting, which increases the design freedom of both tissue culture
platform and engineered tissue construct. This is done by embedding 3D printing of mus-
cle strip formations into alginate-based support gels using ﬁrst ﬂuorescent polystyrene (PS)
beads of size 10 µm to determine the best print path later used for C2C12 cells. The bioprints
were performed in our own fabricated 3D-printed 8-well device. This device includes a PS
substrate covered with a 3D printed biosurﬁne layer, a Styrene-Ethylene-Buthylene-Styrene
(SEBS) + 75% glass spheres ink layer with SEBS + 20% graphite conductive ribbons at-
tached for cell stimulation and maturation. The yield for a successfully working 8-well device
is greater than 83% using this method. All steps of the platform fabrication and tissue inte-
gration are done using 3D printing. The preparation for bioprinting requires an additional
manual loading of support material. The whole process is done at the Technical University
of Denmark (DTU) Health Tech facility.
The theory behind embedded bioprinting, alginate support gel, C2C12 growth and diﬀer-
entiation, and material science (including SEBS, graphite, biosurﬁne, and glass ink) are
examined to understand the principle of muscle strip formations. Additionally, the functions
of the R-gen100 printer were thoroughly examined by running several experiments. This
includes material extrusion (ﬂow rate/ﬂow correction), print path (g-code and SHAPER de-
signs), and robot speed (F).
Initial experiments with C2C12 cells demonstrated higher rates of muscle strip compaction in
alginate slurry with carboxylated Nanoﬁbrillated Cellulose Fibers (cNFC) compared to the
same condition in pure alginate slurry. Initial experiments with 10 µm ﬂuorescent PS beads
were tested in a 10% ﬁbrinogen-based ink and a 10% collagen-based ink formulation. The
ii
==PAGE:4==
ﬁbrinogen-based ink formulation demonstrated higher rates of print path compaction. Ad-
ditionally, an optimal print path was tested using beads for three diﬀerent muscle strip sizes
(1-strand, 4-strands, and 8-strands all in 4 layers), three diﬀerent ﬂow rates (102 nl/mm,
204 nl/mm, and 306 nl/mm) and three diﬀerent robot speeds (1 mm/s, 3 mm/s, and 5
mm/s). Flow rate 204 nl/mm with robot speed 3 mm/s showed promising results regarding
compaction rate, amount of material, and print time.
Removing the support material from the printed constructs was performed after 2 hours
either by enzymatic degradation (using alginate lyase and cellulase) or by using a pipette.
After removal, printed muscle strips with ﬁbrin ink and collagen ink maintained their shape.
C2C12 cells were printed with a speed of 3 mm/s for all three muscle strip sizes with a ﬂow
rate of 204 nl/mm. Muscle strips were printed using a cell concentration of 10 million (mio.)
cells/ml and 20 mio. cells/ml, respectively. All muscle strips held their shape after slurry
removal. Several muscle strips for ﬁbrin ink with 10 mio. cells/ml lost their grip on the rib-
bons and, thereby, their print path shape. Muscle strips with ﬁbrin ink for 20 mio. cells/ml
held their shape and ribbon attachment for longer (>7 days). Collagen printed muscle strips
with 10 mio. cells/ml showed cell spreading after several days (<7 days). Overall yield of
our bioprinting protocol is 87.5%.
By overcoming current limitations, such as high cost and low geometry control, embedded
3D printing oﬀers the potential to generate a cheap method for altering tissue geometry eas-
ily. The intent for the 8-well device is to support one muscle strip per well to demonstrate
a good overview of the eﬀectiveness of the design.
iii
==PAGE:5==
2
Resume
I de senere år er der blevet udført forskning på tredimensionelle (3D) muskel modeller ved
brug af hydrogeler med celler, som støbes i specielt fremstillede forme. Eventuelle designæn-
dringer til dette kræver derfor en forudgående fremstilling af modiﬁcerede forme. I stedet
foreslår vi 3D-printning. Derfor demonstrerer denne afhandling, hvordan man optimerer en
printprotokol til printning af forstadier til muskelvæv. Denne afhandling demonstrerer, hvor-
dan man optimerer en protokol for indlejret print af muskelvævsforløbere. Dette vises ved
at lave indlejret tredimensionel (3D) printning af muskelmodel i alginatbaserede støttegeler
ved først at bruge ﬂuorescerende polystyren (PS) perler af størrelse 10 µm til at bestemme
den bedste printbane, som senere bruges til print af C2C12 celler. Bioprinteren, der blev
brugt, var REGENHU R-Gen100. Bioprintning blev udført i en egenfremstillet 3D-printet
8-brønds platform. Denne platform består af et PS substrat dækket med et 3D-printet bio-
surﬁne lag, et Styren-Ethylen-Buthylen-Styren (SEBS) + 75% glassfære blæklag med SEBS
+ 20% graﬁt ledende bånd tilkoblet for celle stimulation og modning. Udbyttet for en vel-
fungerende 8-brønds platform er større end 83% ved brug af denne metode. Alginat støttegel
tilføjes manuelt ved hjaælp af en pipette ned i de 8 brønde. Hele processen er 3D-fremstillet.
Hele processen blev udført på Danmarks Tekniske Universitet (DTU) Health Tech afdelingen.
For at forstå princippet i at lave muskelmodel, undersøges teorien bag bioprintning, alginat
støttegel, C2C12 vækst og diﬀerentiering samt materialeteknologi (herunder SEBS, graﬁt
og biosurﬁne). Derudover blev funktionerne af R-gen100 printeren grundigt undersøgt ved
at køre ﬂere eksperimenter. Disse inkluderede materialeekstrudering, printbane (g-kode og
SHAPER design) samt robothastighed (F).
Eksperimenter med C2C12 celler viste højere grad af muskel modeller komprimering i algi-
natslurry med karboxylerede nanoﬁbrilleret celluloseﬁbre (cNFC) sammenlignet med samme
forhold i ren alginat slurry. Eksperimenter med 10 µm ﬂuorescerende PS perler blev testet
i en 10% ﬁbrinogen-baseret blæk og en 10% kollagen-baseret blæk formulationer.
Den
ﬁbrinogen-baserede blækformulering viste højere komprimering af printbanen end kollagen.
Derudover blev en optimal printbane testet ved brug af perler for tre forskellige muskel model
størrelser (1 streng 4 lag, 4 strenge 4 lag og 8 strenge 4 lag), tre forskellige ﬂowrater (102
nl/mm, 204 nl/mm og 306 nl/mm) og tre forskellige robothastigheder (1 mm/s, 3 mm/s og
iv
==PAGE:6==
5 mm/s). Flowrate 204 nl/mm med robothastighed 3 mm/s viste lovende resultater med
hensyn til komprimering, mængde af materiale smat printtid.
Fjernelse af støtte materialet fra de printede muskel modeller blev udført efter 2 timer både
ved enzymatisk nedbrydning (ved brug af alginatlyase og cellulase) og ved brug af en pipette.
De printede muskel modeller med både ﬁbrinblæk og kollagenblæk bevarede deres form efter
fjernelse af slurry. C2C12 celler blev printet for alle tre muskel model størrelser med en
ﬂowrate på 204 nl/mm og robothastighed på 3 mm/s. Muskel modellerne blev printet ved
brug af en cellekoncentration på henholdsvis 10 millioner celler/ml og 20 millioner celler/ml.
Alle muskel modellerne holdte deres form efter fjernelse af slurry. Flere muskel modeller med
ﬁbrinblæk og 10 mio. celler/ml mistede deres greb om båndene og derved deres printbane
form. Muskel modeller med ﬁbrinblæk og 20 mio. celler/ml holdt deres form og båndtilknyt-
ning længere (>7 dage). Kollagenprintede muskel modeller med 10 mio. celler/ml viste
celleudbredelse efter nogen dage (<7 dage). Udbyttet for vores bioprint prokotol er 87.5%.
Ved at overvinde nuværende begrænsninger som høj omkostning og lav kontrol over geometri
ændringer, så tilbyder 3D-printning potentialet til at generere en billig metode til let at ændre
af vævets geometri. Formålet med 8-brønds enheden er at understøtte én muskel modeller
pr. brønd for at demonstrere en god oversigt over designets eﬀektivitet. Hensigten med
8-brønd platformen er at understøtte én muskel model per brønd for at demonstrere en god
oversigt over designets eﬀektivitet.

A) The hierarchical structure of a muscle starts with the smallest and inner-
most unit, the multinucleated striated myoﬁber, encased in a basal lamina
that houses satellite cells. These myoﬁbers are then bundled parallelly into
fascicles, each surrounded by a connective tissue layer called the perimysium.
These fascicles are bundled and encased in the epimysium, forming the entire
muscle. B) The development of satellite cells into myoﬁbers and the speciﬁc
myogenic regulatory factors that characterize the steps. [3] . . . . . . . . . .

Madden et al. used Teﬂon masters to generate PDMS molds. A mixture of
myogenic cells and hydrogel proteins was pipetted into the molds enframed
by porous nylon frames. After the bundles had gelled and compacted, the
frames with bundles were removed from the molds for free-ﬂoating culture
(Fig. 1—ﬁgure supplement 2). [4] . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .

Liquid or low-viscosity ink (blue) is extruded at low pressure, leaving a ﬁlm
on the substrate. Shear-thinning inks (orange) ﬂow when the pressure exceeds
the material’s yield stress. Once the stress is removed, the material regains its
original viscosity, allowing ink layers to be stacked to build three-dimensional

==PAGE:21==
4
Introduction
Skeletal muscle, which constitutes a signiﬁcant portion of the human body mass, is crucial
in facilitating respiration, maintaining posture, and enabling locomotion. Healthy skeletal
muscles can regenerate their capacity after small tears, driven by resident muscle stem cells
known as satellite cells (SCs). This allows for recovery from everyday wear and tear. How-
ever, impairments in this self-repair mechanism due to aging, genetic disorders, or volumetric
muscle loss can lead to debilitating myopathies, severely aﬀecting quality of life or shortening
lifespan. [1]
The architecture of skeletal muscle tissue includes highly organized, multinucleated myoﬁbers
aligned in parallel, surrounded by a supportive extracellular matrix (ECM) that provides me-
chanical strength and biochemical signals essential for muscle function and repair. The ECM
comprises proteins such as collagen, laminin, and ﬁbronectin, which create a microenviron-
ment that supports cell adhesion, migration, and diﬀerentiation. [2] Contractility, the ability
of muscle ﬁbers to contract and generate force, is a key feature of functional muscle tissue
and is critical for its role in movement and posture maintenance. This contractile function is
relevant to this thesis as it aims to replicate this physiological characteristic in vitro, thereby
enhancing the model’s relevance for studying muscle physiology and disease. [3]
Traditionally, the study of skeletal muscle biology and the development of therapeutic inter-
ventions have relied on small animal models and two-dimensional (2D) cell cultures. These
models have limitations in accurately predicting human responses to pharmacological com-
pounds. Therefore, there is a growing demand for innovative approaches that combine basic
research with clinical relevance. In vitro models of human tissues have emerged as indispens-
able tools in biomedical research, oﬀering potential beneﬁts for pharmaceutical development
and testing. [2] Additionally, these in vitro models will reduce the reliance on animal stud-
ies, which are not always predictable for human cell behavior, drug eﬀects, and immune
responses. Developing functional muscle tissue models relies on several parameters, such
as material composition and size. Previous studies have successfully created functional 3D
muscle tissues ranging in size from 3 to 11 mm in length, 1 to 4.5 mm in width, and 0.4
to 3 mm in height. These approaches often utilize cast cell-laden hydrogels, a method that
requires prior fabrication of the molds in which the tissue is cast [4][5][6].
1
==PAGE:22==
Micro-extrusion 3D bioprinting is promising for generating 3D models of tissue construct,
including striated muscle. For muscle tissue, contractility is a fundamental characteristic,
representing its ability to contract and generate force in response to stimuli. In vitro mod-
els that exhibit contractile behavior can be used to study muscle physiology, pathology,
and response to various treatments.
Additionally, demonstrating contractility in muscle
models validates that the biofabricated tissue can mimic the dynamic biological properties
of native muscle tissue. Addressing these limitations is crucial for advancing the ﬁeld to-
wards more physiologically relevant in vitro skeletal muscle models, ultimately enhancing
our understanding of muscle pathology and facilitating the development of novel therapeutic
interventions. A challenge with 3D printing is optimizing the environment to support cell
development. Here, the ink composition, cell density, and the print design itself all play
signiﬁcant roles. [5] [7]
As a potential solution, this thesis aims to investigate embedded printing within sacriﬁcial
support matrices to create miniature skeletal muscle models. Embedded 3D printing involves
printing bioink directly into a supportive gel matrix, which can later be removed, allowing
for more intricate and delicate tissue constructs. The C2C12 mouse skeletal muscle cell line
and human skeletal muscle derived cells will be utilized as the model cell source, with varying
concentrations printed and subsequent evaluation through microscopy and measurements.
The aims of this thesis are:
1. To optimize an embedded printing protocol for printing muscle tissue precursors into
granular alginate gel support and alginate gel with cellulose nanoﬁber support, followed
by removing the support gel through enzymatic degradation.
2. To evaluate muscle tissue formation, based degradation on time point, print path, cell
culture time, cell density, and bioink formulation, including content and composition
of extracellular matrix proteins.
The goal is to create a muscle strip resembling the sketch seen in Fig. 1, where C2C12
cells will diﬀerentiate into myotubes and align in the same direction, as well as anchoring
to the two ribbons and thereby forming a dog bone shape when seen from the top/bottom.
Additionally, the device will be constructed from materials that supports cell survival.
2
==PAGE:23==
Figure 1: Sketch of a desired muscle strip formation in the designed 8-well plate with sus-
pended ribbons for cell anchoring.
By addressing these objectives, this research aims to ﬁrst ﬁll the gaps in standardized pro-
tocols for embedded printing of skeletal muscle tissue, focusing on optimizing critical pa-
rameters to achieve functional and physiologically relevant muscle constructs. Secondly, our
ﬁndings aims to contribute to the development of new therapies for muscle diseases or in-
juries. Lastly, the results will help advance the tissue engineering ﬁeld, by providing a fully
functional 3D bioprinting protocol for fabricating small and eﬀective muscle strips for elec-
trical stimulation and drug therapies.
3
==PAGE:24==
5
Theoretical background
This section describes the state of the art within muscle tissue engineering and the theory
needed to understand the embedded 3D printing protocol for printing muscle tissue precur-
sors into diﬀerent granular alginate gels. This includes muscle cell formation, embedded 3D
printing, SHAPER 3D modeling software, and Geometric Code (G-code) editing. These are
important topics for understanding the experimental process and interpreting the results.
This section also describes the state of the art within 3D printing and the theory needed in
order to understand device fabrication for muscle tissue engineering purposes.
5.1
Myogenesis and tissue engineering
Skeletal muscles are hierarchical structures described at three levels, as illustrated in Fig.
2A. The ﬁrst level is the smallest and innermost unit: the striated myoﬁber, a long, multin-
ucleated muscle cell. A myoﬁber contains myoﬁbrils composed of long chains of the proteins
actin and myosin. When these proteins slide past each other, the myoﬁber contracts in a
single uniaxial direction. Each myoﬁber is encased in a part of connective tissue called basal
lamina (extracellular scaﬀold), housing progenitor cells, so-called satellite cells, which are
crucial for maintaining the myoﬁbers. The second level is the fascicles, which consist of
myoﬁbers aligned parallelly in bundles to maximize contraction strength. Each fascicle is
surrounded by a connective tissue membrane called the perimysium. The third level is the
entire muscle, which consists of bundles of fascicles surrounded by the epimysium, another
sheath of connective tissue. The epimysium continues into the tendon, which attaches the
muscle to the bone. [3]
4
==PAGE:25==
Figure 2: A) The hierarchical structure of a muscle starts with the smallest and innermost
unit, the multinucleated striated myoﬁber, encased in a basal lamina that houses satellite
cells. These myoﬁbers are then bundled parallelly into fascicles, each surrounded by a con-
nective tissue layer called the perimysium. These fascicles are bundled and encased in the
epimysium, forming the entire muscle. B) The development of satellite cells into myoﬁbers
and the speciﬁc myogenic regulatory factors that characterize the steps. [3]
Fig. 2B illustrates the development of SCs into myoﬁbers and the speciﬁc myogenic regu-
latory factors that characterize the steps. Satellite cells ﬁrst become undiﬀerentiated my-
oblasts. These myoblasts then diﬀerentiate into myocytes, which fuse to form myotubes.
And ﬁnally, myotubes mature into myoﬁbers. The developmental stage can be identiﬁed by
molecular characterization techniques such as western blots, immunostaining and polymeric
chain reaction. Myoblast determination protein 1 (MyoD) is expressed by undiﬀerentiated
myoblasts before fusion, while Myogenin is upregulated in functional myoﬁbers.
Myosin
Heavy Chain (MHC) conﬁrms the presence of more matured myoﬁbers. [3]
Several external factors inﬂuence the muscle development.
Myoﬁbers detect mechanical
signals from the ECM by using proteins called integrins, which can form complex trans-
membrane bundles. These complexes amplify and transmit external mechanical stimuli and
directly impact the remodeling of the actin cytoskeleton and the mechanical properties of
5
==PAGE:26==
the myoﬁbers. In this way, the cell obtains signals of matrix stiﬀness or shear stress. [3]
Native murine skeletal muscle tissue has an elastic modulus in the range of 10-50 kPa [8]
[1] [9] [10]. When muscle cells contract, they generate tensile and compressive stress within
the tissue. Shear stress is not the primary type of stress during contractions, however there
can be shear stress due to ﬁlament movements relative to their ECM. This internal mechan-
ical stress can inﬂuence cell behavior like growth and diﬀerentiation. Shear stress is more
commonly associated with endothelial cells in the blood vessels when blood ﬂows. In muscle
tissue in vascularized areas, the ﬂow of interstitial ﬂuids, inﬂuenced by muscle contractions,
can create shear stress. Shear stress occurs when a force is applied parallel or tangential to a
surface, as opposed to normal stress, which occurs due to forces being applied perpendicular
to a surface. [11]
In vivo, skeletal muscle undergoes passive stretching due to its attachment to bones and
body movements. Studies have shown increased diﬀerentiation when in vitro tissue is often
fastened between two points (e.g., on posts or pillars) or applying controlled stretching,
resulting in an anisotropic environment that promotes uniaxially aligned myoﬁbers. [12]
[13] An external electrical stimulation by neurons plays a crucial role in regulating muscle
growth, diﬀerentiation, and adaptation.
Another external factor is chemical signals like
growth factors. [3]
5.1.1
The extracellular matrix
The ECM is essential for tissue development and structural support, particularly in skeletal
muscle. The basal lamina (part of the ECM), includes a laminin-rich matrix and collagen
IV, supports cell proliferation and diﬀerentiation. Surrounding the basal lamina is the intra-
muscular connective tissue (iMCT), composed mainly of collagen I, which transmits forces
to the tendon for muscle movement. The ECM also facilitates the diﬀusion of oxygen and
nutrients, retains water to maintain hydration, and provides cell attachment sites via inte-
grins and adhesion molecules. It guides cell alignment, which is crucial for organized tissue
structures like muscles. Additionally, cells actively remodel the ECM by secreting enzymes
and producing new ECM components, ensuring the dynamic adaptation necessary for tissue
growth, repair, and maintenance. [1]
6
==PAGE:27==
5.1.2
The C2C12 cell line as a model for human muscle
While primary human myoblasts and SCs are ideal for generating physiologically relevant
muscle tissue, their use in initial optimization studies can be challenging due to limited avail-
ability and complex culture requirements. Therefore, this study employs the C2C12 mouse
myoblast cell line as a model system. C2C12 myoblasts derived from mice have proven eﬀec-
tive as a model for myogenesis and myotube diﬀerentiation studies. The cells diﬀerentiate
willingly and rapidly, express characteristic muscle proteins, and produce contracting my-
otubes[14][15]. C2C12 cells exhibit a high level of the ﬁbrinolytic enzyme plasminogen, which
can pose a challenge in constructing C2C12 muscle strips based on a ﬁbrin structure[14].
While 2D cell culture oﬀers precise control over the cell microenvironment and facilitates
rapid drug screening, traditional muscle cell cultures result in immature myotubes with lim-
ited physiological relevance, partly due to stiﬀsubstrates like glass or plastic. It has been
demonstrated that cells are highly sensitive to their physical environment, and culturing
myotubes on softer substrates that mimic muscle stiﬀness, 10-50 kPa [1] [9] [10], improves
their structure. Despite eﬀorts to enhance cell adhesion using ECM proteins, rigid 2D sub-
strates still hinder myotubes contractile ability and fail to replicate the native muscle’s 3D
complexity. Delamination of cells from their substrates during long culture periods is an im-
portant reason why maturation is limited in 2D cultures. Therefore, engineering a biomimetic
3D muscle environment in vitro could address these limitations, allowing for longer culture
times, larger myotube size, increased protein content, and improved maturation of MHC
gene expression compared to 2D cultures. 3D cultures overcome this limitation by utilizing
biomimetic materials often enriched with ECM proteins like matrigel, which enhance cell
adhesion and provide a more supportive environment for cell growth and diﬀerentiation.
Extended culture periods are possible because the cells are more stable and healthier in an
environment that resembles their natural setting. Additionally, 3D cultures are better at
promoting the development of muscle cells to a state that more closely resembles that found
in vivo therefore there is a larger MHC expression. [1]
Maintaining a density of C2C12 cells (50-60% conﬂuence) is crucial to prevent contact inhibi-
tion to prevent premature diﬀerentiation. Therefore, routine passaging and cryopreservation
are necessary to maintain the proliferative capacity of the cells and prevent spontaneous
7
==PAGE:28==
diﬀerentiation. [16]
5.2
State of the art
This section describes the method currently used for casting 3D muscle strips and the cur-
rently available platforms for stimulating muscle strips. The size of the described engineered
muscle strips on the mm-scale is comparable to the tissues designed in this thesis. The plat-
forms in which the tissues in this thesis are engineered can be expanded to provide electric
stimulation.
5.2.1
Myobundles engineered by molding
Bundles of contractile muscle tissue have been engineered by the hydrogel molding technique
illustrated in Fig. 3. This process involves several steps: ﬁrstly, designing a Teﬂon master
mold, into which a Polydimethylsiloxane (PDMS) mold is cast. The resulting PDMS mold
features a semi-cylindrical hole intended for the hydrogel and a rectangular groove to ac-
commodate a laser-cut, porous nylon frame in which the ends of the cast myobundles are
secured. The inner dimensions of the nylon frame are 6.8 x 8.3 mm. Each frame is designed
to hold two muscle bundles positioned side by side. [4]
8
==PAGE:29==
Figure 3: Madden et al.
used Teﬂon masters to generate PDMS molds.
A mixture of
myogenic cells and hydrogel proteins was pipetted into the molds enframed by porous nylon
frames. After the bundles had gelled and compacted, the frames with bundles were removed
from the molds for free-ﬂoating culture (Fig. 1—ﬁgure supplement 2). [4]
After the muscle bundles had been compacting for 3-5 days and diﬀerentiating for another
3-5 days, the primary human myogenic cells began to twitch spontaneously. To measure
stimulated contractile force generation, the nylon frame with the tissue was transferred to
a custom force measurement bath to measure twitch and tetanic force in the bundles. One
end of a muscle bundle was pinned to an immobile PDMS block, and the other end was
attached to a PDMS ﬂoat connected to a force transducer mounted on a computer-controlled
motorized linear actuator. To allow the actuator to stretch the bundle, the sides of the nylon
frame were cut open.[4]. Khodabukus et al. used this method to produce human muscle
tissue and found that electrical stimulation promoted growth and structural maturation of
myobundles. They applied 1-week electrical ﬁeld stimulation to myobundles that had been
diﬀerentiated for 7 days[2].
9
==PAGE:30==
5.2.2
Biowire
Zhao et al. [5] demonstrated a scalable in vitro platform for culturing human cardiac tissues.
Their platform, called Biowire II, integrates multiple materials to create a 96-well plate that
combines tissue anchorage, electrical stimulation, and contraction sensing. The engineered
cardiac tissues were created within the dimensions of the microwells, which were each 5 mm
× 1 mm × 0.2 mm (length × width × height). The elastic microwires used as anchoring
points for the tissues had a diameter of 0.1 mm. These microwires were suspended across
the microwells and served both as anchor points and force sensors. Based on their imaging,
there is a spacing of 3 mm between their suspended ribbons. The system is shown in Fig. 4.
Figure 4: Biowire system showing cardiac myotube in red suspended between polymer wires
shown in brown and carbon electrodes shown in black. [5] Scale bar 1 mm.
For the tissue to eﬀectively deﬂect the wires, they need to be thin and soft.
They are
functioning as force sensors as the deﬂection provides feedback to the muscle tissue. The
wires are cast to be 100 µm in diameter and cured to a modulus of 33 kPa [5]. Speciﬁcally,
it has been shown that human myoblasts in 2D culture require a substrate with an elastic
modulus around 15 kPa to diﬀerentiate optimally and form myotubes [17].
5.3
Curi Bio’s Mantarray platform
The muscle cells are embedded in a hydrogel matrix and cast into a mold like base plate with
wells in deﬁned dimensions using the Mantarray platform. This casting process creates a 3D
structure of the muscle tissue. These constructs are typically allowed to mature for a given
period of time, during which the cells align and develop into functional muscle tissue. The
10
==PAGE:31==
Mantarray system uses magnetic sensors to measure the contractile forces generated by the
engineered muscle tissues in real time. This allows for high-throughput analysis of muscle
function, providing valuable data on the mechanical properties of the tissues. It also has
electrodes to apply electrical stimulation to the 3D bundles to induce contractions. Magnetic
sensors measure contractile force by converting magnetic ﬁeld strength change while distance
change between magnets in the poles. The platform enables various functional assays, in-
cluding measurements of contractions, fatigue resistance, and responses to pharmacological
agents. These assays are crucial for evaluating the muscle tissue’s physiological and phar-
macological properties. [18] [6]
The Mantarray system ﬁts a standard 24-well plate. The system can be seen in Fig. 5
Figure 5: A) Curi Bio Mantarray system. B) 3D casted muscle tissue within a standard
24-well plate. C) Zoom in of the muscle tissue. Scale bar 1 mm on image C. [6] [18]
5.4
3D Printing for Device Fabrication and Tissue Engineering
The tissue generation techniques described above provide eﬀective methods for engineering
muscle tissues. However, muscle tissue engineering is an evolving ﬁeld that requires optimiza-
11
==PAGE:32==
tion regarding cell survival and maturation, nutrient and oxygen diﬀusion, and mechanical
properties. It would therefore be desirable to have the ﬂexibility to easily modify tissue de-
signs. However, both of the described methods have the inherent limitation that new molds
need to be produced if the size or shape of the muscle tissue needs to be changed. The pro-
duction of molds and casting of plates are time-consuming and can be omitted if the tissue
is generated using 3D printing techniques instead of casting. Editing a 3D printed design
is quick and easy with computer-aided design (CAD). This process eliminates the time and
material needed to create new molds.
5.4.1
Direct Ink Writing for device fabrication
In extrusion 3D printing, also known as Direct Ink Writing (DIW), structures are created
by extruding and depositing material onto a substrate. A liquid or low-viscosity ink can
be extruded at low pressure to form a ﬁlm on the substrate. Some materials maintain a
consistent viscosity until a minimum stress threshold, known as the yield stress, is surpassed.
At yield stress, the internal micro-structural formations of the material change, allowing it
to ﬂow. These are shear-thinning materials and are often used to achieve a 3D shape. Once
the shearing force is removed, shear-thinning materials regain their viscoelastic properties.
This characteristic enables the ink strands to be layered, as illustrated by the orange ink in
Fig. 6. [19]
Figure 6: Liquid or low-viscosity ink (blue) is extruded at low pressure, leaving a ﬁlm on the
substrate. Shear-thinning inks (orange) ﬂow when the pressure exceeds the material’s yield
stress. Once the stress is removed, the material regains its original viscosity, allowing ink
layers to be stacked to build three-dimensional structures. Illustration from C. Radeke [19]
12
==PAGE:33==
5.4.2
Free standing prints using SEBS
Rheological properties and printability using SEBS composite inks with carbon particles
have previously been reported in literature [20]. Fig. 7A) shows a 1 Hz amplitude oscillation
sweep and ﬂow sweep of SEBS 1:2 weight to weight ratio with 20% carbon black (CB) par-
ticles. The storage modulus (G’), representing the elastic component, quantiﬁes the energy
stored within the material, reﬂecting its solid-like characteristics. Higher G’ values indicate
that the material behaves more like a solid. Conversely, the loss modulus (G”), representing
the viscous component, measures the energy lost, highlighting its liquid-like properties. The
higher the G” values, the more the material exhibits liquid-like behavior.
The rheological behavior demonstrates that the ink can transition between solid-like and
liquid-like states depending on the applied stress.
This is crucial for maintaining shape
ﬁdelity during printing and achieving free-standing structures. Fig. 7B) shows an example
of free-standing SEBS. Adding particles to ink compositions can give inks with shear thinning
abilities, which is helpful for free-standing prints. The ink’s viscosity decreases under shear
stress (during extrusion), allowing it to ﬂow through the nozzle. Once the shear is removed
(post-extrusion), the viscosity increases, helping the material to hold its shape.
Figure 7: A) G’ and G” curve. B) Freestanding prints using CB particles and SEBS. Scale
bar 1 mm
Butyl acetate (ButAc) is used as a carrier solvent for SEBS in the formulation of the inks.
It enables the creation of thin, compliant traces essential for ﬂexible electronics and multi-
13
==PAGE:34==
material printing. Their work highlights that the shape ﬁdelity of the printed structures is
ensured by even evaporation of the butyl acetate solvent. Rapid evaporation of the solvent
helps achieve and maintain the printed shape. [20]
5.5
Deﬂection of a ﬁxed beam
When working with suspended ribbons in the experimental setup for this thesis, the ribbons
can be considered as a ﬁxed beam, as seen in Fig. 8. The equations in this section stem
from [21].
Figure 8: Schematic representation of a ﬁxed beam with length L and an applied load F at
the center point.
The deﬂection δ of a ﬁxed beam is given by:
δ = −Fx2
48EI (3L −4x)
(1)
Where I = ⇡r4
4 is the moment of inertia for the beams cross-sectional area of the beam with
radius r. F is the load applied to the midpoint of the beam, x is the the position along the
length of the beam, L is the total length of the beam and E is the modulus of elasticity
of the beam. Eq. 6 is used to calculate the deﬂection of the beam at any point x along
the beam. The term
Fx2
48EI is the deﬂection factor, where EI is the rigidity of the beam and
(3L −4x) adjusts the deﬂection based on the position along the beam.
The maximum deﬂection of a ﬁxed beam occurs at x = L
2 and is given by:
δmax =
FL3
192EI
(2)
14
==PAGE:35==
Eq. 2 shows that the deﬂection is proportional to the applied load F.
The formula for the deﬂection of a ﬁxed beam can be related to the concept of the spring con-
stant (or stiﬀness) of the beam, which is a measure of how much the beam resists deformation
under an applied load. The spring constant k is deﬁned using Hooke’s Law:
F = kδ
(3)
Where F = the applied force, δ = the displacement (deﬂection) and k = the spring constant
(or stiﬀness). Rearringing Eq. 2 and solving for F:
F = δmax · 192EI
L3
(4)
Comparing this to Hooke’s Law in Eq. 3, we get the spring constant k for the beam to be:
k = 192EI
L3
(5)
A higher k means the beam is stiﬀer and resists deﬂection more, while a lower k means the
beam is more ﬂexible and deﬂects more easily under the same load. Higher E and higher I
will increase the spring constant, making the beam deﬂection smaller, where as a higher L
of the beam will decrease k, making the beam deﬂect more.
5.6
Embedded 3D bioprint
In tissue generation, the bioink material comprises a hydrogel containing a mix of cells
and diﬀerent molecules. A low-viscosity hydrogel can be preferred to minimize cell damage
during printing. However, this low viscosity makes building a stable 3D structure challenging.
Embedded 3D bioprinting oﬀers a solution to this problem. In this technique, low-viscosity
bioinks are printed into a supportive gel bath, which helps maintain the printed shape of the
path. An example is in Fig. 9, where a blue structure is printed in a pink, transparent gel
support bath. [22]
15
==PAGE:36==
Figure 9: Embedding the printed path in a support bath allows printing with low-viscosity
hydrogel ink. The structure of the embedded blue ink is supported by the pink alginate gel
bath. Scale bar 5 mm
The gel support used in this process can be a so-called sacriﬁcial support.
This means
that after the supported structure is polymerized or cross-linked, the viscosity of the ink
increases, allowing the three-dimensional structure to maintain its shape without the support.
Subsequently, the support gel is removed or degraded.
5.6.1
Jammed, granular hydrogels as support gel
Granular hydrogels,composed of jammed hydrogel granules, can be used as support gels in
embedded 3D printing. These hydrogels are biocompatible materials which provide a highly
hydrated environment for cells. When these hydrogels are granulated into randomly loose-
packed, microscale particles larger than 10 µm, and the particle-to-volume fraction reaches
0.58-0.64, a jamming transition occurs. These jamming granular hydrogels act as viscous
ﬂuids under shear stress and revert to a viscoelastic solid once the external load is removed.
Fig. 10 illustrates how the shear-thinning-like behavior of the jammed, granular hydrogels
creates a self-annealing matrix, where the shear of the needle creates a groove in the gel
where the low-viscosity ink can be deposited. [23]
16
==PAGE:37==
Figure 10: When the needle moves through the jammed, microscale granular hydrogel par-
ticles, they exhibit behavior similar to shear-thinning. As the needle applies shear force to
the particles before it (green-edged particles), they ﬂow, creating a groove for ink deposition
(yellow). Once the shear force is removed, the hydrogel re-solidiﬁes (blue-edged particles),
supporting the printed path [23]. Adapted from [23]. Created with BioRender.com
An example of a commercialized granular hydrogel is Carbopol ETD (Easy To Disperse)
2020, a synthetic, biocompatible, biodegradable poly (acrylic acid) polymer. Interactions
between charged poly(acrylic acid) polymer particles and ions in cell growth media can alter
rheological behavior and negatively aﬀect cell performance. An example of biocompatible,
natural polymer-based granular hydrogels is the development of freeform reversible embed-
ding of suspended hydrogels (FRESH), where gelatin is bulk-formed and blended into a
slurry with desired rheological properties. Alternatives to gelatin include granular hydrogel
support baths made of agarose, gellan or alginate. These materials are cytocompatible, easy
to remove without leaving signiﬁcant residues, and do not interfere with the printed cells. [23]
Self-healing Annealable Particle-Extracellular Matrix (SHAPE) is another jammed granular
microparticle system in which alginate gel is mixed with collagen. C. Radeke incorporated
cNFC ﬁbers in a SHAPE slurry and discovered that adding 2% cNFC led to greater com-
paction of the printed material. This increased compaction can be beneﬁcial in engineering
muscle tissue, as it ensures physical contact between myocytes, thereby enhancing myotube
formation.
17
==PAGE:38==
5.7
Bioink formulations
It has been shown that C2C12 cells both proliferate and diﬀerentiate within collagen hy-
drogels. Collagen I is the most abundant ECM protein in native skeletal muscle. However,
healthy myoﬁbers directly engage with basal lamina proteins. This has led to using Matrigel
to enhance tissue formation. High collagen levels can lead to low contractile forces, which
means there will be poor muscle regeneration and function. Additionally, collagen I is diﬃ-
cult to remodel, as it does not stimulate natural ECM production and may not provide the
optimal stiﬀness for muscle diﬀerentiation. [1]
Fibrin can be extensively remodeled, degraded, and replaced by endogenously derived ECM.
Therefore, it is a preferred matrix for tissue engineering of skeletal muscle over collagen.
Fibrin also has a stiﬀness that is more comparable to that of native muscle. Additionally,
ﬁbrin promotes angiogenesis and neurite extension, which are important for fully functional
engineered muscle formation in vitro. Adding Matrigel to the ﬁbrin gel increases force gen-
eration capability. Fibrin is considered better than collagen for muscle tissue engineering
because, under similar conditions, collagen’s high passive stiﬀness leads to premature rup-
turing of strongly contracting engineered muscle tissues. This suggests that ﬁbrin is superior
to collagen due to its ability to support muscle tissue engineering without causing premature
rupture. [1]
The stiﬀness of both collagen and ﬁbrin can be manipulated by adjusting the precursor
monomer concentration and/or changing the concentration of the cross-linking agent. The
literature reports that collagen stiﬀness values range from 200-800 MPa, and ﬁbrin stiﬀness
values range from 0-5000 MPa. [8]
18
==PAGE:39==
6
Materials and methods
The following sections describe the laboratory work done at DTU Health Tech (Department
of Health Technology).
This includes the fabrication of the 8-well device, the materials
investigated for the support bath and ink choices for embedded 3D bioprinting of C2C12
cells and human primary skeletal muscle cells.
6.1
Device fabrication
Printing the ribbon devices using DIW 3D printing have previously been studied by another
master student Jason Chan from the TMAT group at DTU Health Tech [24]. Firstly, he
found that printing suspended ribbons across a 3 mm gap did not work well and that printing
them suspended for 9 mm and 6 mm was possible. However, printing at 6 mm was more
feasible. Therefore, our ribbons span 6 mm from anchor point to anchor point across the
well. We have two ribbons that span 6 mm each through the well and that are spaced 3
mm apart. Secondly, he proposed that instead of having several muscle strips in one well
across the same ribbons, that each well should have their own set of ribbons with only one
construct. Lastly, he tested several conditions for the best conductivity on our graphite rib-
bons, and he found that 37% w/w SEBS:ButAc and 20% v/v Graphite:SEBS had an elastic
modulus around 7.76 MPa. This has established the foundation for the design of our ribbon
platform. The platform is constructed by following our own ribbon device protocol, which
can be seen in App. 13.3. This Appendix also shows the protocol for cleaning SEBS (SEBS,
KRATON G1645) in App. 13.4.
On the device fabrication process we closely collaborated with Jonathan B. Fjendsbo. The
process can be seen in Fig. 11.
19
==PAGE:40==
Figure 11: Printing process of the 8-well ribbon device. Biosurﬁne is printed ﬁrst, then the
substrate is UV cured. Next a layer of glass ink is printed, then the conductive ribbons and
lastly a gasket layer mad (37%) SEBS for sealing the well. The ﬁnal device has 8 wells.
Created with BioRender.com
The substrate was held into place in the RegenHU 3D Discovery G5 printer using our own
PS holder, the same holder which was later used for holding the 8-well device in place while
printing the muscle strips in the R-gen 100 bioprinter. The holder ensures consistent device
placement across machines, facilitating easier alignment easier. The holder can be seem in
Fig. 12.
20
==PAGE:41==
Figure 12: Our substrate holder. a) Substrate seen while 3D printing the device. b) substrate
with slurry placed in the bioprinter.
A PS substrate slide of size 25 mm x 75 mm of 1.5 mm thickness were UV-Ozone-treated for
10 minutes (NanoBioAnlytics UV Ozone Cleaner UVC-1014). All the 3D printed aspects of
the device was printed on RegenHU 3D Discovery G5 printer. After oxidization biosurﬁne
(Tokyo Gosei, BIOSURFINE TM) was printed at speciﬁc sites on the substrate in eight 6
mm x6 mm squares with center-to-center spacing of 12.8 mm (print settings include: speed
10 mm/s, plunger velocity 0.005 mm/s, ﬂow rate 0.0836 µl/s, initial height 20 µm and re-
tract distance disabled). Biosurﬁne was added to prevent cell growth at the bottom of the
wells. Biosurﬁne was mixed at ratio 1:1:1.5 (Biorsurﬁne:MiliQ water:Ethanol 96%). The bio-
surﬁne was loaded into a 1 ml Hamilton syringe and printed with a 60 µm needle (Cellink,
NZ5340505001). After printing biosurﬁne the substrates ware brought to a UV cure box
(Wicked Engineering CUREbox UV) and cured for 30 minutes at room temperature. After
curing (either on the same day, or next day) the glass ink layer can be printed on. This layer
was made of SEBS cleaned with Soxhlet extraction in ButAc (33%) w/w) with 75%) w/w
silanized glass spheres (CAS: 65997-17-3, Sigma Aldrich Product No: 440345). This layer
was printed with a 510 µm nozzle (Cellink, Cat No.: NZ4200005001) at ⇠150 kPa pressure.
Following the glass layer the ribbons (made from SEBS cleaned with Soxhlet extraction in
ButAc (37% w/w) with 20% w/w graphite of 1 µm size) were printed with a 200 µm noz-
zle at ⇠400 kPa. The graphite used was from nanograﬁ(SKU NG04EO072379). Lastly, a
gasket layer was printed using SEBS cleaned with Soxhlet extraction in ButAc (37%) w/w).
This layer acts both as a glue for attaching the 8 wells and as a seal to prevent leaking
21
==PAGE:42==
from the device. The wells and lids was made from PS slides (330 mm x 250 mm sheet of 2
mm thick PS (stored at 60◦C)), which were vacuum formed at 180◦C using (Vaquform DT2,
A ) on aluminium molds of size of 25 mm x 50 mm, each having 8 wells Ø8 mm, with a
center-to-center spacing of 12.8 mm. Lids were made from a 25 mm x 50 mm PS slide as
well and had a center-to-center spacing of 12.8 mm, but were made with Ø9.7 mm to ﬁt
around the wells. The lids were removed from the mold 5 minutes after forming and wells
were cut out using Flux BeamBox Laser cutter (settings: mm PS was 60% power at speed
8 mm/s.) The ﬁnal device can be seen in Fig. 13.
Figure 13: Final 8-well ribbon device showing the 8 wells with suspended ribbons 3 mm
apart.
To make the design of our muscle strip ﬁt the design of the ribbon platform we needed to
know the height of the ribbons within each well. We know from printing the devices there
was deviation in the ink depending on how long it has been out, small weighing diﬀerences
and pressure diﬀerences when printing. Therefore, we measured the approximate height of
our ribbons by measuring the glass ink layer. We measured two diﬀerent places on the same
substrate (by A1 and by B4) and found that there is a deviation in the height both between
substrates and within the same substrate. The measurements were performed using one of
the R-GEN 100’s print heads with a 400 µm needle as seen in Fig. 14A). The overview of
where we measured can be seen in Fig. 14B).
22
==PAGE:43==
Figure 14: A) Measurements took place at A1 and B4 with a 400 µm needle. B) Overview
of how we label the wells in our 8-well device. Measurements took place at A1 and B4.
To perform the bioprints knowing the ribbon height was essential. Therefore, the substrate
height itself was measured ﬁrst, then the glass ink layer was measured at A1 og B4, respec-
tively. The diﬀerence between these two measurements were found and the approximate
ribbon height was determined. The average was found to be 0.475 mm. Tab. 1 shows the
measurements.

The fabrication process of the 8-well devices took multiple days, as it includes soxhlet ex-
traction of SEBS, preparation of PS slide and vacuum forming and laser cutting of wells
and lid. However, once everything is prepared, printing one device takes ⇠10 minutes. The
g-code ﬁles can be seen in App. 14.1. The graphite ink, the glass ink and the gasket ink
were all made at once right before printing.

==PAGE:44==
6.2
Muscle strip design
A lot of thought has been given into designing our muscle strips. We aimed to develop a
innovative way of easily controlling placement and geometry by using 3D bioprint of the
muscle strips compared to state of the art which cast muscle strips. A comparison can be
seen in Tab. 2, where the calculations for our bioprints can be seen in App. 15.2. Our
muscle strips are designed in several layers to achieve optimal height and length for ribbon
attachment as well as to achieve a width that prevents breaking.
An initial path is drawn in SHAPER REGENHU (version 1.6.4.0). SHAPER has limited
drawing capabilities and the output code in .bioﬁle is fairly large (400+ lines for simple
drawing designs). Our SHAPER drawing experiments can be seen in App. 16. The use of
SHAPER gives a quick substrate deﬁnition and easy way of controlling sequential printing
of all 8 wells and good material deﬁnition properties. The .bioﬁle is imported to Pangolin
(printer interface) and exported as a .cn ﬁle. This ﬁle is directly altered using G-code to
deﬁne printing parameters.

6.3
C2C12 cell line
The used cell line for muscle strip formation experiments was the C2C12 mouse myoblast cell
line. The cells were handled according to the protocol in App. 13.1. All C2C12 cultures were
performed in sterile conditions and incubated at 37◦C, 100% humidity, 5% CO2. They were
cultured in Dulbeccos Modiﬁed Eagle Media (DMEM) with 10% Fetal Bovine Serum (FBS)
and 1% Penicillin/Streptomycin (P/S). The medium was heated to 37◦C before changing.
Media was changed every other day during culture. Cells were passaged and harvested at
80% conﬂuency. The passage numbers for our cell line were in the range of p21-p34. Cells
were frozen down in cryovials with 4 mio/cells per vial in 0.9 ml FBS and 0.1 ml DMSO
(Dimethyl sulfoxide). One vial of our cell stock was diﬀerentiated on day 4 in DMEM with
2% Horse Serum (HS) and 1% P/S to follow diﬀerentiation properties.
6.4
Human skeletal cell line
The human cell stock was provided by our supervisor Asli Aybike Doğan from Novo Nordisk.
The protocol for media formulations and handling can be seen in App. 13.7. Human cells
were not used for initial experiments during this thesis, and they will not be referred to
until the ﬁnal experiments within this report. The cells used are Skeletal Muscle-Derived
Cells, which are cryopreserved vial of single donor, myoblast-like, non-diﬀerentiated primary
human muscle cells.
6.5
Bioink formulations
The bioink formulatation used can be seen in Tab. 3. The procedure for making the bioinks
can be seen in our protocol in App. 13.5. The ECM components in the bioink mixture were
mixed in a sterile speed mixer cup to ensure neutralization of bioink. Cells were added by
gently pipetting the mixture. The containers were spun at 2500 rpm for 5-10 seconds in a
DAC 150.1 FVZ speed mixer to ensure even distribution.

following the Curi Bio testing guide for thrombin and ﬁbrinogen. Following their guide we
mixed 137 µl media, 10 µl ﬁbrinogen and 3 µl thrombin, added it to a sterile Petri dish and
incubated for 10 minutes. After incubation a ﬁrm gel was formed that could be scooped up
and handled for the new stock, where as the old stock was still watery.

Support material choices
The support material choices can be seen in Tab. 4. The procedure for making alginate
support bath slurry and alginate support bath slurry with cNFC20 8x can be seen in our

==PAGE:48==
protocol in App. 13.2 and App. 13.5.
Table 4: Alginate slurry formulations for collagen and ﬁbrin ink, respectively.
Alginate slurry
for Fibrin ink (4.5ml in total)

3.0 ml Alginate (Wako, 80⇠120) (66.7%)
3.0 ml Alginate (Wako, 80⇠120) (66.7%)
150 µl Thrombin
(1000 U/ml) (3.3%)
(Curi bio, SIGMA: T4648-1KU )
1.5 ml cNFC (33.3%)
(from 5% stock)
600 µl DMEM + 1% P/S (13.3%)
750 µl cNFC (16.7%)
(from 5% stock)
The cNFC were freeze dried with the help of supervisors within the TMAT group. After
freeze drying the ﬁbers were weighed and correct amount of media added in the ratio of
1:20 (cNFC20 8x:media). The ﬁbers were soaked overnight and homogenized the next day
at 10.000 rpm for 5 minutes with a T25 Ultra-Turrax homogenizer (IKA) equipped with
S25N-10G dispersing tool.
The micro jammed alginate particles were made by dissolving 1 g of Sodium Alginate (Wako,
80⇠120) in water in a 100 ml glass jar. The solution was stirred for 2 hours on a magnetic
stirrer in a 60°C water bath. Equal volumes of sterile 2 mg/ml CaCO3 solution and alginate
solution were mixed and stirred at room temperature for 2 hours. Acetic acid (99.9%) was
added at a 1:500 ratio and stirred vigorously (650 rpm) for 4 hours. The gelled solution
was homogenized at 15.000 rpm for 10 minutes using an Ultra-Turrax homogenizer with a
T25-S25N-10G dispersing tool. These solution was centrifuged at 18.500 G for 20 minutes
in a sterile falcon tube. The supernatant was discarded, and the particles were resuspended
in DMEM + 1% P/S in the ratio of 1:2 (slurry:media). The pH was adjusted to 7 with
2 mM NAOH. Afterwards the solution was homogenized at 15.000 rpm for 3 minutes and
centrifuged at 18.500 G for 10 minutes. The supernatant was discarded, forming a pellet of
jammed alginate microgel. To keep the solution sterile 0.5% Chlorofom was added.
28
==PAGE:49==
6.6.1
Slurry degradation
Removal of the slurry is done using enzymatic degradation of cellulose and alginate. DMEM
+ P/S is supplemented with 0.01 U/ml Alginate Lyase (A1603-100MG 10.000 units/gram
solid, SIGMA-ALDRICH, CAS-no.
9024-15-1) and 5 U/ml Cellulase (Aspergillus niger,
Tokyo Chemical Industry Co. LTD, Cas: 9012-54-8).
The pH values for the bioinks and slurry was tested to ensure it was neutral for bioprinting
studies. Everything was within range. The values can be seen in App. 16.
6.7
Print protocol for bioprinting
A schematic representation of what is required for bioprinting can be seen in Fig. 15. Cells
are kept at -135◦C and stored until use. The cells are then thawed and added to the ink
formulation and loaded into a needle, which is placed in the printer. The cleaned and ready
to use 8-well devices are loaded with alginate/cNFC composite slurry and placed in the 3D
printer. Once printed, the devices are stored in the incubator for optimal cell conditions.
Fig. 15 only demonstrates ﬁbrin ink and corresponding slurry using C2C12 cells, however
the procedure is the same for collagen ink with C2C12 cells, or human skeletal cells in either
of the ink formulations.
Every bioprint was performed using the R-gen 100 bioprinter and follows the preparations
in our protocol seen in App. 13.5 and our R-gen 100 protocol seen in App. 13.6.

direction to get same amount of material above and below the ribbons and Fig. 17, where it
is illustrated from the top how the needle pushes the ribbons when printing just 1 strand in 4
layers. The movement is the same for when we used 4 strands 4 layers and 8 strands 4 layers.

6.8
Fluorescent imaging and microscopy
Images of the ﬂuorescent beads and print paths were taken using a Zeiss Observer Z1 micro-
scope equipped with a mounted Zeiss AxioCam. Utilizing the ZEN software, the camera was
conﬁgured to detect only wavelengths corresponding to the ﬂuorescent beads (505-515 nm),
along with a bright ﬁeld image to see the ribbon placement in the well. Additionally, some
prints were imaged using the z-stack function, and the resulting z-stacks were generated in
Zenlite/ImageJ (Fiji). When imaging cell prints, the Zeiss Primovert microscope (415510-
1101-000) mounted with an IDS GmbH camera (UI-3240ML) was used and the software used
to capture the images was the uEye Cockpit.

==PAGE:54==
7
Initial experiments and results
This section brieﬂy describes the results of the device fabrication process. Then it describes
all the experiments we performed and a brief comment of the results, which led to the choice
of the bioink formulations, support gel, print path, and other relevant choices to obtain the
ideal muscle strip. Only the most important results are shown and compared, whereas other
relevant results are shown in the App. 18.
7.1
8-well ribbon platform
The fabrication process of the 8-well device will give between ﬁve to eight successful wells
out of the eight wells on one device. Therefore, the yield is greater than 83%. Periodic
variations in the ink mixture can lead to ﬂuctuations in printing pressure. It is, therefore,
necessary to monitor the printing process while printing to ensure successful production.
The success varies depending on parameters such as pressure, time, alignment, etc. Some
parameters might have to be adjusted momentarily within the process because of variations
in the device. The height of the glass ink tends to slightly vary; thus, to ensure good ribbon
attachment, this height might have to be corrected during the printing process. The result
of a 4-5 hours printing process is shown in Fig. 19.
Figure 19: Result of device production showing the 8-well platforms.
A total of 40/48
successful wells (yield of 83%). Scale bar 25 mm.
34
==PAGE:55==
7.2
Alginate vs. alginate with cNFC ﬁbers as a support gel
To select the optimal support gel for C2C12, we tested a simple muscle strip in both alginate
and alginate + cNFC ﬁbers. The printed muscle strip includes 3 passes. The muscle strips
can be seen in Fig. 20.
Figure 20: A) C2C12 muscle strip in alginate slurry. B) C2C12 muscle strip in alginate
slurry + cnfc. Scale bar 200 µm.
The C2C12 cell muscle strip is more compact (mean width 174.1 µm for alginate with cNFC
versus 346.7 µm for pure alginate slurry) in the support gel with cNFC than in pure algi-
nate. At the center, the muscle strip in the cNFC support gel is more compact, whereas the
center in pure alginate is wavy, and cells are migrating away from the strip. In pure alginate
support, cells also tend to escape the printed muscle strip more easily and migrate toward
the end of the wells. Adding cNFC to the slurry might act like a viscosity modiﬁer, and
its ﬁbrous network and negative charge help hold the print in place and push cells in the
printing line, aiding the compaction of prints rather than resulting in dispersing. [19]
In conclusion, the alginate + cNFC ﬁber supper gel will be used for all other experiments.
We expect it to provide the best support for our print and the best results for maintaining
cell proliferation and diﬀerentiation within the strip.
35
==PAGE:56==
7.3
Collagen vs. ﬁbrin ink suspension
When testing a speciﬁc condition, we print once per well but also replicate across the entire
well plate, allowing us to observe the eﬀects of varying conditions simultaneously.
This
approach has been chosen for ease of device tracking and labeling in Petri dishes and prevent
cross-contamination of ink, slurry, etc., between wells within each device. This approach
demonstrates repeatability and ensures an overall good assessment. Furthermore, it is faster
to print in all 8-wells at once when inserted into the printer. We made conclusions based
on the collective evidence of multiple trials rather than relying on a singular event. Fig. 21
shows how a print with beads can look like in the 8-well device.
Figure 21: The 8-well device showing beads prints in alginate slurry.
In Fig. 22 we see an experiment to test collagen ink conditions vs. ﬁbrin ink conditions in
alginate + cNFC support gel. The ﬁbrin ink has 450 µl beads in it, and the collagen ink
has 100 µl beads in it. It is worth mentioning that the increase in beads in the ﬁbrin ink
might aﬀect the viscosity of the construct and thereby the compaction. However, it is still
very clear that the beads appear much more compact in the ﬁbrin ink than in the collagen
ink. In the collagen ink, we also see beads escaping the center line, whereas the beads stay
within the center line for the ﬁbrin ink. This could make ﬁbrin ink the desired choice for
achieving the optimal muscle strip design. These observations are conﬁrmed by measuring
the average width across printed strips. The average width for collagen ink strips is 715 µm,
where as for ﬁbrin ink strips it is 570 µm. The measured values can be seen in App. 16.
36
==PAGE:57==
(a) Beads in collagen ink. Wells A2, B3 and B4.
(b) Beads in ﬁbrin ink. Wells A2, A4 and B3
Figure 22: Prints in ﬁbrin ink turned out to be more compact than collagen ink. The average
width of collagen is 715 µm, and for ﬁbrin, it is 569 µm. The dotted lines shows indicate
the width of the outermost beads. The average was calculated based on eight wells of each
ink type. Scale bars 1000 µm.
7.4
Print path parameters
Experiments for diﬀerent ﬂow rates and diﬀerent spacing between passes can be seen in App.
16. We have deﬁned our boundary conditions by deﬁning ﬂow rates based on desired/de-
ﬁned number of cells and bundle size and minimum printing time with suﬃcient printing
resolution. Therefore, we have chosen to experiment with ﬂow rates 102 nl/mm, 204 nl/mm
and 306 nl/mm going forward for a 120 µm overlap between layers. The printing time is
not critical for beads, but will be for cells. The 120 µm spacing for 8 passes in 4 layers took
approximately 23 minutes for 8 wells for robot speed 1 mm/s. To optimize this we included
the robot speed as a parameter in our experiments.

designs can be seen in Tab. 5, Tab 6 and Tab. 7.

7.5
Analysis of the 1-strand, 4-strand, and 8-strand bead strip sizes
In App. 16 an overview of the results from the conditions in Tab. 5, Tab 6 and Tab. 7
can be seen. In Fig. 23, the 1-strand, 4-strand, and 8-strand with ﬂow rate 204 nl/mm and
robot speed 3 mm/s can be seen.
38
==PAGE:59==
Figure 23: A) 1-strand strip. B) 4-strand strip. C) 8-strand strip. All conditions are ﬂow
rate 204 nl/mm and robot speed 3 mm/s. Scale bars 500 µm.
It can be seen both from App. 16 and Fig. 23 that all the conditions follow the coded print
path and that the prints maintain their shape within the slurry support. Additionally, it
is demonstrated that the muscle strip size can be easily changed and give wider structures
depending on the desired shape of the constructs. The theoretical dimensions of the 1-strand
construct for ﬂow rate 102 nl/mm is estimated from Tab. 2 to be 0.32 mm x 5 mm (W x L),
for the 4-strand it is estimated to be 0.92 mm x 5mm (W x L) and for the 8-strand 1.72 mm
x 5 mm (W x L). The measured values for the strands under diﬀerent ﬂow rates and robot
speeds can also be seen in App. 16. The measured width value for ﬂow rate 102 nl/mm
for the 1-strand is 0.2mm, for the 4-strand 0.7 mm, and for the 8-strand 1.88 mm. There
is some deviation between the theoretical values and the actual printed construct. For the
1-strand there is a 37.5% deviation, for the 4-strand there is a 23.91% and for the 8-strand
there is a 9.30% deviation. The widths are inﬂuenced by several parameters including, ink
formulation, tear in the material from the robot speed/needle and incubation time, so a
deviation is expected. The theoretical lengths of all strips are 5 mm and the lengths of the
strips seen in Fig. 23 are A) 4.3 mm, B) 5.0 mm and C) 5.8 mm. The mean of the printed
length is approximately 5.03 mm. The standard deviation, which indicates the variation
from the mean, is about 0.61 mm. The range of our measurements indicates that the diﬀer-
ence between the maximum and minimum values is 1.5 mm.
39
==PAGE:60==
Fig. 24 shows the Z-stacks for the 1-strand, 4-strand, and 8-strand. It is clear that the
material surrounds the ribbons.
Figure 24: Z-stacks of the printed bead strips. A) 1-strand. B) 4-strand and C) 8-strand.
Blue dots indicate the approximate ribbon placement. Each cube in the grid is 600 µm x
600 µm
When considering cells, we prefer to minimize the print time while maintaining as low as
possible stress on the cells. Flow-rate and robot speed both have an aﬀect on the cells when
they are being extruded. Based on the above results, we have chosen to move on with the
sizes 1-strand 4 layers, 4-strands 4 layers and 8-strand 4 layers for ﬂow rate 204nl/mm and
robot speed 3mm/s for printing with cells.
Additionally, we wanted to see how the ribbons aﬀect the printed construct and if the rib-
bons push away material when the needle is pushing the ribbons. Therefore, we printed the
conditions in a well with ribbons and a well without ribbons to see the eﬀect. Fig. 25 shows
the results of that experiment.
40
==PAGE:61==
Figure 25: A1 and A2) showing the top view of a 4-strand 4 layers strip with attachment to
ribbons. B1 and B2) showing the top view of a 4-strand 4 layers strip without ribbons. C)
showing a 4-strand 4 layers strip with a ribbon in one end and not the other. Scale bar 500
µm.
It is evident that the ribbons inﬂuenced the printed construct. Clearly seen in Fig. 25 B1,
B2 and C, the part of the printed construct that does not have a ribbon is shaped in a more
straight way, while the strips in A1 and A2 are wider outside the ribbon. Measurements
support that the strip outside the ribbon is approximately 50% wider than in the width
halfway between the ribbons.
Strips without ribbons does not increase the width more
than 20%. This makes sense since the extruded material without ribbons is not aﬀected by
anything other than the needle pulling slightly through the material. Where as, the side
with a ribbon is aﬀected by the needle and the movement of the ribbon due to the needle
moving it. As demonstrated earlier in Fig. 16 and Fig. 17, the needle pushed the ribbon
from side to side, leaving it to move through extruded material several times in one print.
The aﬀect of the ribbons leave for more material to attach to a wider ﬁeld of the ribbon
which is good for cell anchoring to the ribbons, thus forming the desired dog-bone shaped.
41
==PAGE:62==
7.5.1
Degradation time points of support gel
During our optimization experiments, we observed some inconsistency due to lack of poly-
merization between ﬁbrinogen and thrombin. This is consistent with the lot-to-lot variability
mentioned earlier. We did trouble shooting and found the source of the problem. These ex-
periments can be seen in App. 16.
7.6
Fibrin and collagen prints
Slurry was removed both by pipetting and using enzymatic (Alginate lyase and Cellulase)
degradation. This was done for several robot speeds, which can be seen in App. 16. Addi-
tionally, Amino Caproic Acid (ACA) was added to the media formulations. ACA prolongs
the integrity and durability of the ﬁbrin matrix in tissue constructs. By inhibiting ﬁbrinol-
ysis. ACA helps maintain the structural stability of the engineered tissue, which is crucial
for tissues that require longer times to mature. [2] As seen in Fig. 26 the printed lumps are
strong enough to undergo manual pipetting of the slurry and they held their shape when
using enzymatic degradation both for collagen and ﬁbrin. The enzymatic degradation is
preferred as there is no chance of manually breaking the construct. It can also be seen that
the strips are pulled towards the side of the well where the pipette was positioned.
Figure 26: A1) Fibrin printed 4-strand in slurry. A2) Fibrin printed 4-strand after pipetting
slurry out. A3) Fibrin printed 4-strand in slurry. A4) Fibrin printed 4 strand after enzy-
matic removal. B1) Collagen printed 4-strand in slurry. B2) Collagen printed 4-strand after
pipetting slurry out. B3) Collagen printed 4-strand in slurry. B4) Collagen printed 4-strand
after enzymatic removal. Scale bar 500 µm
42
==PAGE:63==
8
Experiments with C2C12 cells
This section presents the results of the selected ﬁnal conditions for the muscle strips, serving
as a proof of concept. The chosen ﬂow rate was 204 nl/mm and the robot speed was 3 mm/s.
The printed muscle strips can be seen in Fig. 27. It is clear that the printed constructs are
placed at the center of the wells and that there is a distinct diﬀerence in sizes.
Figure 27: Printed muscle strips with C2C12 cells in ﬁbrin ink. Dotted lines added around
the muscle strips. A) 1-strand 4 layers construct. B) 4-strand 4 layers construct. C) 8-
strands 4 layers construct. Scale bars 5 mm.
From multiple cell experiments, we found that slurry can be removed from both collagen
and ﬁbrin ink prints using either a pipette or enzymatic degradation. This can be seen in
App. 17. Therefore, the results in the next section are not analyzed based on the chosen
slurry removal method. The time line for when growth media, enzymes (Alginate lyase and
Cellulase) and diﬀerentiation media is added for the muscle strips, and is shown in App. 17.
8.1
Fibrin muscle strip
For ﬁbrin muscle strips with 10 mio. cells/ml a total of 24 muscle strips (8x 1-strands, 8x
4-strand and 8x 8-strand) were printed. Out of these 24 muscle strips 23 had ribbon anchor-
ing on day 1. This gives a yield of 95.8%. Out of these 23 strips, 18 strips survived until
day 7 and 18 survived until day 14, which gives a yield of 78.2% on day 14. Loss of muscle
strips is likely due to weak anchoring to the ribbons, which means the strip got misaligned
during compaction of cells during diﬀerentiation.
43
==PAGE:64==
The ﬁgures below shows a representative from each muscle strip size (1-strand, 4-strand and
8-strand. Fig. 28 shows the 1-strand muscle strip from day 1, day 7 and day 14 in culture.
Figure 28: Printed 1-strand muscle strips with C2C12 cells in ﬁbrin ink with 10 mio. cells/ml.
Scale bar 500 µm.
Fig. 29 shows the 4-strand muscle strip from day 1, day 7 and day 14 in culture. Unfortu-
nately, the strip lost its grip to one of the ribbons before day 7, likely due to cell compaction.

8.2.1
Height and width measurements
For all the muscle strips that had ribbon anchoring, their width and heights were measured
on day 1, 7 and 14.
The measurements can be seen in table in App.
17.4 and shrink-
age/expansion over time in App. 17.5. The number of strips are indicated by n, and the
mean width, mean length and standard deviations are calculated in mm. The pixel to µm
calculations can be seen in App. 15.2 and the deviation calculations in percentage and plots
are made in Matlab, which are seen in App. 19. The measurements for heights and widths
for day 1, 7 and 14 are plotted in Fig. 37 for the collagen muscle strips with 10 mio. cell-
s/ml, ﬁbrin muscle strips with 10 mio. cells/ml and ﬁbrin muscle strips with 20 mio. cells/ml.
The key observation from Fig. 37A1), B1) and C1) is the decrease in mean heights starting
from day 1 to 14 for all the conditions. For ﬁbrin (10 mio. cell/ml) the mean length for
the 1-strand on day 1 is 5.5 mm, where as on day 14 it is 4.0 mm (28% shrinkage). For
the 4-strand the height decreases from 5.4 mm on day 1 to 4.1 mm on day 14, while for
the 8-strand, it reduces from 5.5 mm on day 1 to 4.2 mm on day 14. For ﬁbrin (20 mio.
cells/ml) the mean height for the 1-strand goes from 4.9 mm on day 1 to 3.7 mm on day 14,
the 4-strand from 4.5 mm on day 1 to 4.0 mm on day 14, and the 8-strand from 4.1 mm on
50
==PAGE:71==
day 1 to 4.0 mm on day 14. For collagen strips the mean height goes for the 1-strand from
5.1 mm on day 1 to 3.8 mm on day 7, the 4-strand goes from 5.2 mm on day 1 to 3.3 mm
on day 14, and the 8-strand from 5.4 mm on day 1 to 4.5 mm on day 7 (the 1-strand and
8-strand did not survive until day 14). It is worth mentioning that the print path for the
length is coded to be 5 mm. Both collagen and ﬁbrin (10 mio. cells/ml) length values are
about 5 mm on day 1. For ﬁbrin (20 mio. cells/ml) the length on day 1 is mainly between
4.0-4.8 mm, which is a little smaller, we expect that this is due to higher density of cells.
Since imaging was done 2 hours after printing the muscle strips have already compacted.
From Fig. 37A2), B2) and C2), we can see that the mean widths of the muscle strips varies
over time. For the collagen 1-strand the mean width on day 1 was 0.4 mm and on day 7
it was 0.5 mm. For the 4-strand it was 1.1 mm on day 1 and 0.8 mm on day 14, and the
8-strand was 2.3 mm on day 1 and 2.8 mm on day 14. There a general increase in width,
which makes sense since slurry is no longer supporting the printed construct. For ﬁbrin (10
mio. cells/ml) the 1-strand mean width was 0.5 mm on day 1 and 0.5 mm on day 14, for
the 4-strand it was 1.4 mm on day 1 and 1.5 mm on day 14. The 8-strand was 2.5 mm on
day 1 and 1.8 mm on day 14. The 1-strand and 4-strand showed no change in mean width,
whereas the 8-strand exhibited a decrease. This could be due to ﬁbrin compacting the prints
more than collagen. For ﬁbrin (20 mio. cells/ml) 1-strand the mean width on day 1 was 0.5
mm and 0.4 mm on day 14. The 4-strand was 1.9 mm on day 1 and 1.6 mm on day 14. The
8-strand was 2.4 mm on day 1 and 2.4 mm on day 14. For the 20 mio. cells/ml the mean
width appear either constant or decreasing slightly, which could be due to higher density
of cells. In general the ﬁbrin muscle strips maintain their width better than the collagen
muscle strips. The printed construct is coded to have a width of 0.32 mm for the 1-strand,
0.92 mm for the 4-strand and 1.72 mm for the 8-strand. The measured values are slightly
higher than this.

==PAGE:73==
To conclude, the results in this section proved that printing a muscle strip using embedded
3D bioprinting in alginate slurry + cNFC ﬁbers in diﬀerent sizes is possible. Additionally,
it is possible to remove slurry by using enzymatic degradation or a pipette. There are no
cross-linking issues with collagen or between ﬁbrinogen and thrombin at the concentrations
we used. The printed muscle strips were evaluated on collective observations of multiple
trials for the same conditions, and not only on a singular event.
9
Experiments with human muscle cells
Human muscle cells were printed with ﬁbrin ink with a cell density of 20 mio. cells/ml in
alginate + cNFC support slurry. The muscle strips can be seen in Fig. 38, Fig. 39 and Fig.
40. We were on able to collect data from day 1 and day 5 in culture and measure a single
muscle strip for each size. On day 1, the 1-strand had a length of 4.6 mm, where as on day 5
it was 4.5 mm. The width for the 1-strand was 0.5 mm on day 1 and 0.6 mm on day 5. For
day 1, the 4-strand had a length of 4.9 mm, where as on day 5 it was 3.5 mm. The width
for the 4-strand was 2.0 mm on day 1 and 1.2 mm on day 5. For day 1, the 8-strand had a
length of 5.2 mm, which had decreased to 2.3 mm on day 5. The width for the 8-strand was
2.3 mm on day 1 and 2.1 mm on day 5. In general the lengths and widths were observed to
decrease. This is likely due to the compaction of the cells.
