10
Discussion
This thesis investigated the embedded 3D printing of muscle precursors into alginate support
both with and without cNFC support materials followed by degradation of support gels
within our fabricated 8 well ribbon device. The eﬀectiveness of this method will be discussed
in relation to polymer microspherical ﬂuorescent beads, C2C12 murine cells and human cells,
including the inﬂuential parameters.
10.1
The ribbon platform
Optimizing the ribbon platform was a part of this project, as ribbon height signiﬁcantly
inﬂuences bioprinting success. The ribbon height varied depending on the material used for
ribbon anchoring when doing 3D device prints. Initially, pure 37% SEBS did exhibit con-
sistent adherence across diﬀerent inks. This led to variations in ribbon height both within
the same device and across devices. The decision to incorporate glass spheres into the ink
resulted in less variation in the ribbon height, that now could be consistently expected within
the same µm range. This facilitated the design of bioprinting paths to ensure tissue anchor-
ing to the ribbons.
Optimization points for device production include stabilizing the ink formulations, determin-
ing the order of printing layers, adjusting pressure values and SEBS preparation. According
to the literature, a ribbon size of 100 µm is desired[5], whereas ours is in the size range of
⇠200 µm. This can be mitigated by printing with a smaller nozzle/needle size. As optimiz-
ing ribbons falls outside our objectives, we have chosen to proceed with a 200 µm ribbon to
evaluate the muscle strip formation and survival. Previous work within the research group
has demonstrated cell anchoring to the ribbons. Our current work utilizes the same ribbon
design[24].
It can be discussed how suﬃcient the fabricated 8-well device is, as it might not hold enough
media. Over time, we have observed leakage from the devices. They are very dependent on
who and how the vacuum wells are pressed on. The SEBS ink formulations also vary daily,
resulting in daily pressure diﬀerences. The pressure diﬀerences greatly aﬀects the extrusion
of material, which could lead to ribbons not being free-standing. The print time is short,
approximately 10 minutes per device, whereas, the preparation time is lengthy due to the
56
==PAGE:77==
involvement of multiple necessary steps. Furthermore, the printer cannot be started and left
unattended, even though everything (after biosurﬁne) is printed in one go from the same
ﬁle. There are too many variations and parameters to monitor. These include pressure vari-
ations, nozzle and needle clogging, height deviations, bumps/unevenness within the printed
construct, ribbon attachment issues, and alignment issues in general. The 8 well device do
have a great function upon bioprinting as many muscle strips can be printed at once, however
this is also true for a commercial 6-well plate, where several muscle strips can ﬁt within one
well. Conversely, the 8-well device requires less slurry than the 6-well plate, which is relevant
for both price and preparation time.
When maintaining the devices in culture for extended periods (7-14 days) we did experience
some leakage issues from certain wells. The leakage occurred randomly in some devices but
not in others. When a device leaked it was sometimes only one well and sometimes multiple
wells. It was diﬃcult to determine which were leaking, as the slurry support did not leak,
only the media formulations added later. Sometimes we did not observe leakage initially,
however after 1-2 days in culture, leakage had occurred. The percentage of leaking wells
were not counted/tracked systematically, as in the interest of time we only continued using
wells with muscle strips in them. The muscle strips in the leaking wells were terminated as
we did not add media to them after the initial observation of leakage. However the entire
device was kept in culture, as the non-leaking wells remained functional.
10.1.1
The suspended ribbons
Our suspended ribbons can be compared to a rod ﬁxed at both ends. When the ribbons
undergo pulling from the tissue construct it can be compared to deﬂection of a ﬁxed beam.
We anchor muscle strips to the center and would like to see the ribbons deﬂection. The
moment of inertia given by I = ⇡r4
4 , which is proportional to the radius raised to the power
of 4. This means that even small changes in the radius will have a signiﬁcant eﬀect on I
(resistance to the rotation), if the value of I becomes smaller due to smaller r of the ribbons,
then the beam will deﬂect more, as seen again from Eq. 6:
δ = −Fx2
48EI (3L −4x)
(6)
Comparing the literature our ribbons, of 6 mm, are longer than e.g. Zhao et al [5] (Biowire
57
==PAGE:78==
system), where their ribbons are 1 mm long.
The modulus of the Biowire ribbons are
signiﬁcantly softer. If the diameter of our ribbons where smaller it would lie within the same
modulus range. It is desired that the beams are able to deﬂect, since anchoring of tissue to
stiﬀmaterial will increase the change of tissue breakage/delamination. Our ribbon stiﬀness
was calculated by Jason Chan to have an elastic modulus of 7.8±0.8 MPa. The Biowire
systems ribbons have a modulus of 33±3 kPa.
10.2
Print path design
Another challenge has been modifying the G-code directly within the R-gen printing software.
This was not feasible for a large part of the project timeline, consequently, our ﬁrst bioprints
were done with the .bioﬁle exported directly from SHAPER. Changing the design in the
code was a major challenge, as a three-layer strip drawn in SHAPER gave us a 400+ line
script in the .bioﬁle. Controlling the print direction, the muscle strip size, and the extruded
material posed a challenge. The software was then updated and enabled to convert a .bioﬁle
to a .cn ﬁle, with a manageable code, where it was feasible to change x-, y-, and z-direction
within minutes (the g-code is seen in App. 14). Moreover, controlling the ﬂow rate became
an option, whereas previously, we only had the ability to adjust ﬂow without indication of
volume or unit. Furthermore, changing the motor speed directly in the G-code was also
possible. This provided us with a more eﬀective method for optimizing and investigating
individual print parameters.
10.3
Print parameters
Optimizing an embedded printing protocol for depositing muscle tissue precursors into gran-
ular alginate gel with cNFC ﬁbers turned out to be a signiﬁcant task. This was mainly
because there were many unknown parameters to evaluate, and running several experiments
in parallel could quickly make the task overwhelming. We sought to be as systematic as
possible and optimize one parameter at a time. We quickly decided to choose alginate with
cNFC ﬁbers based on two printed 8-well devices with cells, as seen earlier in Fig. 20, where
the average width was approximately 50% smaller. Furthermore, we made a quick decision
to choose more extensive research with the ﬁbrin ink over the collagen ink based on Fig.
22, where the average width was approximately 20% smaller for ﬁbrin strips compared to
collagen. The ﬁrst challenge was, as mentioned, hitting the right ribbon height to ensure
58
==PAGE:79==
equal amounts of biomaterial on either side of the ribbons. Once this challenge was solved,
we aimed to deﬁne an easy print path controller, a possibility that came with a new software
update. Once we could control this, we tested diﬀerent design sizes, the 1-layer, 4-layer, and
8-layer. From Tab. 2, we know that other muscle strip designs have a volume of between
2-150 µl, whereas ours have between 2-17 µl depending on using the 1-strand, 4-strand, or
8-strand. Which suited our idea of making the strips smaller than current methods. We also
did not know how compaction with cells would aﬀect the prints. We would also like to avoid
necking due to cells’ plastic deformation of the material, by having ribbons that can deﬂect
and enough material sustaining the anchoring to the ribbons. It should be mentioned that
the ink formulation for the ribbons has not been our aim and that we have seen necking
of the printed muscle strips (for all sizes), likely due to a too high stiﬀness of the ribbons.
Other strips lost their anchoring to one or more ribbons. If the ribbons had been less stiﬀ,
the increased ﬂexibility might have allowed them to follow the compaction of the tissue,
preventing it from slipping oﬀ.
It is also worth mentioning that the components must touch for ﬁbrinogen to polymerize
with thrombin. Since thrombin is in the slurry, thrombin must diﬀuse through the slurry to
reach the ﬁbrinogen in the printed tissue construct. The time for this to happen is estimated
from previous experiments by our supervisor Asli to be 80 minutes. However, whether this
is suﬃcient for thrombin to reach through our 8-strand design or just through our 1-strand
design can be discussed. When ﬁbrin tissues are cast, one thrombin-containing gel is mixed
with the ﬁbrinogen-containing gel in the casting moment [4]. This ensures a thorough mixture
of thrombin and ﬁbrinogen, resulting in shorter diﬀusion distances compared to our device.
10.4
Muscle strip sizes
We kept the needle diameter constant throughout all our experiments, which meant our
way of testing diﬀerent muscle bundle sizes varied from ﬂow rate and the number of passes
(1-strand, 4-strand, or 8-strand). The initial length and height of the bundles were kept
constant at 5 mm x 0.92 mm (height x width). After incubation, size and compactness
varied depending on cell density, bioink formulation, slurry composition, and culture time.
59
==PAGE:80==
10.4.1
Muscle strip formation
Most studies of in vitro tissue culture evaluate cell proliferation and diﬀerentiation in 2D
using C2C12. However, this is not ideal for mimicking the complexity of in vivo tissues.
When the undiﬀerentiated myoblasts are printed, they are all round and distributed homo-
geneously throughout the construct. Cells are imaged at diﬀerent time points during the
culture (1, 7, 14, and 21 days). Day 7 C2C12 cells are mainly round without merging into
myotubes yet. [25]
To achieve the goal of evaluating muscle tissue formation in our muscle strips based on
parameters like cell density, degradation of support material, print path, and bioink formu-
lation, it was crucial for us to maintain a suﬃcient stock of C2C12 cells. These cells must
exhibit optimal proliferation in growth media and optimal diﬀerentiation in diﬀerentiation
media. It is essential for us to avoid printing with pre-diﬀerentiated cells or a cell stock
mainly composed of dead cells. Therefore, ensuring conﬂuency and proper handling during
seeding is vital.
Throughout the project, we improved the quality of the seeded cells, thereby gradually
achieving higher overall survival rates. Microscopy observed the initial formation of my-
otubes where the cells were anchored to the ribbons. These positive changes were due to
two factors: seeding new C2C12 cells with a lower passage number (p23 instead of p34) and
becoming more precise in splitting the cells at lower conﬂuency levels to prevent cells from
touching each other and pre-diﬀerentiate. The durability of the printed tissue also improved.
The transition from observing dissolved ﬁbrin structures with high cell dispersion from the
tissue into the well to achieving more compact, durable structures involved resolving cross-
linking issues caused by insuﬃcient enzymatic activity in the thrombin stock. Khodabukus
et al. report that ﬁbrin’s disadvantage as a scaﬀold is lot-to-lot variability and variable
degradation rate. This issue can be addressed by testing the polymerization before printing
and adding ACA to control ﬁbrinolysis post-printing[1]. When we changed the protocol, so
ACA was added to the media in both proliferation and diﬀerentiation phases, the quality
and compactness of the printed ﬁbrin structures improved signiﬁcantly, even after removing
the supporting slurry. ACA was added from day 1 (the printing day) until the ﬁrst diﬀeren-
tiation day (day 4).
60
==PAGE:81==
Cell-loaded collagen-based versus ﬁbrin-based bioink were tested. Only 11% of collagen strips
could be maintained until day 14, whereas 78% of the ﬁbrin strips still was anchored to both
ribbons in their well. This can be seen in App. 17. The collagen type in the ink is collagen
I, the most abundant ECM component in native skeletal muscles. Other studies state that
collagen I do not promote the secretion of endogenous ECM, and it may not oﬀer the ideal
stiﬀness for muscle diﬀerentiation[8]. Myoﬁbers in native tissue interact directly with the
basal lamina, which is rich in collagen type IV[1]. This type is added to the collagen ink as a
component of Geltrex. According to Khodabukus et al., a high collagen content negatively
impacts the functional maturation of muscle due to cells’ diﬃculties in remodeling collagen I.
[1]. Therefore, a way to improve the maturation and stability of muscle tissue using collagen
bioinks might be to decrease the amount of collagen I while increasing the amount of Geltrex.
Comparing length in muscle strips printed with cell density 10 mio. cells/ml to muscle strips
printed with 20 mio. cells/ml resulted in shorter muscle strips on both day 1 and day 14 in the
strips with higher cell density. This is seen in App. 17. This can result from the changed ink
viscosity due to the double cell amount. The fact that the ﬁbrin bioink with 20 mio. cells/ml
was printed ﬁve days after the ﬁbrin bioink with 10 mio. cells/ml can also be a reason due
to minor diﬀerences in slurry or bioink ingredients. The increased compactness of the strips
with 20 mio. cells/ml may be preferable, as C2C12 myoblasts should make physical contact
to initiate myotube formation. This supported high cell density in the subsequent tests with
human cells. In literature varieing cell densities have been used. Zhengwei Lia et al. used 2.5
mio C2C12 cells/ml casting gel to obtain cyclic muscle contractions, both spontaneous and
electrically stimulated[26]. This thesis evaluate the muscle strips by measuring compaction.
In literature, tissues are evaluated by force of contractions or genetic expression of regulatory
factors[2] [4] [5].
10.4.2
Maintenance of the printed constructs
When comparing our media amount within our wells in the 8-well device, one well can hold
up to 500 µl of media. We print muscle strips in 200 µl slurry and add 200 µl media on top.
Once the slurry is fully degraded (app. 12 hours later), it is removed, and we add 400 µl of
media. Whereas when seeding cells in 2D we give 25 ml of media to 3.45 mio cells (in T175
ﬂask) - this gives 7.25 µl media per cell. For our 4-strand 4 layer muscle strip for ﬂow rate
61
==PAGE:82==
204 nl/mm, one strip has 170.000 cells, which gives 2.9 µl media available per cell. Even
though the metabolism of cells in 2D cannot be directly compared to 3D, this could indicate
that the wells need to be enlarged. Larger wells could mean that change of the medium can
be reduced to every other day during the proliferation phase. Another solution could be
redesigning the tissue to approximately half its current size.
10.5
Substrate material stiﬀness
Stiﬀness of the support material is a key consideration when trying to achieve optimal com-
paction of 3D muscle tissue cultures. Literature demonstrates at 10-50 kPa [9] [10] [1] cells
have good diﬀerentiation. We do not have measurements of our stiﬀness, and thus are not
sure the gel formation has had an ideal stiﬀness for cells in 3D. When looking through a
microscope we expect to see a good degree of alignment in our muscle strip and an increase
in myotube formation at day 14[1].
When checking cells in the muscle strips using the
bright-ﬁeld microscope available it has been challenging to see whether good diﬀerentiation
and myotube formation has happened, since the construct are so dense and challenging to
illuminate through. We would aim to do some molecular characterization analysis but time
was limited. We would also suggest diﬀerent microscopy methodologies, micro-slicing the
tissue or modifying the design to make it compatible with microscopy, e.g., redesigning the 1
strand 4 layer strips into a 4 strand 1 layer strip. We were able to perform length and width
measurements of our muscle strips over time and, at the same time, look at shape variation
between prints and breakage over time. Section 8 highlights the constructs that maintain
their shape in culture, wheres as App. 18 demonstrates some of the broken construct.
10.6
Support gel
Using alginate with cNFC ﬁbers as a support gel for bioprinting has demonstrated promising
results during this thesis and using this composition would be our recommendation for future
studies using our protocol. In [24] the slurry compositions consisted of 2:1 ratio of alginate
slurry to collagen, however in his study the support gel was not removed, which meant the
cross-linked support slurry held the constructs nicely. Since we had to remove the slurry
composition, we wanted a slurry which were able to compress the printed construct and able
to be removed after 2 hours after printing. Therefore, we choose a 2:1 ratio of slurry with
cNFC ﬁbers, consistent with the ﬁndings of [19], where 2.5% cNFC in SHAPE increases the
62
==PAGE:83==
compaction of the printed constructs. The compaction is important for cellular fusion during
diﬀerentiation of cells.
The support gel is only present in the ﬁrst critical ours, during printing and 2 hour incubation
for cross-linking of collagen or polymerization of ﬁbrinogen and thrombin. Hereafter, the cells
themselves take over and must anchor to the ﬁbrin network/collagen network and each other.
10.7
Human cells
We had a motivation to create a more physiologically relevant model in our platform. There-
fore, we aimed to test human cells as well to validate the approach. Human primary muscle
skeletal cell strips are not extensively discussed, as they are still in culture while we write.
The human cells are from the passage p10 and had a viability of 98%. It was expected that
the human cells would diﬀerentiate and grow better than the C2C12 cells, however within
the time scope, this was not evaluated. Human cells were printed with robot speed 3 mm/s,
ﬂow rate 204 nl/mm and for the 1-, 4- and 8-strand. Since we only had 20 mio. human cells,
we could only do one bioprint with these. Unfortunately, our ink had a lot of bubbles after
mixing, which may have disturbed the print paths on day 1. On day 5 the bubbles have
disappeared. The muscle strips have been measured and their width and heights correspond
to the theoretical values presented earlier in Tab. 2. The theoretical value for the length was
5 mm and on day 1 the average width across all sizes was 4.9 mm. The strip will be further
evaluated after this thesis.
63
==PAGE:84==
11
Conclusion
This thesis investigated the optimization of an embedded printing protocol for printing mus-
cle tissue precursors into alginate support gels. A ribbon platform for muscle tissue anchoring
and stimulation was made using multi-material 3D printing. SEBS with 20% volume graphite
ink demonstrated suspended ribbons with diameter sizes of ⇠200 µm. An advantage of 3D
printing devices like our 8-well platform is its relatively cheap cost as well as quick produc-
tion time. The 8-well devices demonstrated a yield greater than 83 % when looking at fully
functioning ribbons within the wells. The working wells with intact ribbons had no signs of
breaking upon use and the same devices for dummy print with 10 µm ﬂuorescent beads lasted
several cleanings and incubation times. The bioprinting protocol was successfully optimized
using beads under a lot of diﬀerent conditions. For all conditions the needle diameter size
was kept constant at 400µm. Using a ﬂow rate of 204 nl/mm showed promising results for
optimal muscle strip sizes and volumes. The tested sizes included our 1-strand, 4-strand and
8-strand with volumes of 2.1 µl, 8.5 µl and 16.7 µl, respectively, for a ﬂow rate of 102 nl/mm.
Robot speeds of 1 mm/s, 3 mm/s were tested throughout this thesis. Robot speed 1 mm/s
was too time consuming for the 4-strand and 8-strand, as when multiplying the time per
well by 8, would be a long time for the cells. The 1-strand demonstrated by far the fastest
printing times regardless of the robot speed and the size of the tissue is more important than
the speed of the printer.
Additionally, this thesis investigated how to remove support gels by enzymatic degradation.
The optimal slurry was found to be alginate gel + cNFC as it demonstrated larger com-
paction, where the mean width of a printed 1-strand construct was 174.1 µm in alginate
with cNFC versus 346.7 µm for a printed 1-strand construct in pure alginate slurry. This
included comparing muscle strips with enzymatic removal, manual pipette removal and time
of removal (within hours or within days). The time point for slurry degradation was only
evaluated after determining the print path parameters. Therefore, the cross-linking issue
was found late in the project time line. However, is has been successfully demonstrated with
both collagen and ﬁbrin ink formulations that the muscle strips hold their shape after slurry
degradation with both beads and cells. The investigated time points for slurry degradation
included 2 hours after printing and several days after printing. Supportive test with adding
media on top of slurry were also performed, however for increased cell survival the fastest
64
==PAGE:85==
removal time point was found to be 2 hours after printing. Using enzymatic removal of slurry
2 hours after print was the most promising method, as there was no chance of mechanically
destroying the muscle strip.
Lastly, this thesis aimed to investigate cell-culture time, cell density and bioink formulations,
and ECM composition. The tested ink formulations were a Collagen type I based ink and
a ﬁbrin ink (based on ﬁbrinogen and thrombin polymerization). In early experiments for
printed beads strips under the same conditions, the average width of the print path was
23% smaller for ﬁbrin than collagen, therefore ﬁbrin ink was the desired choice. However,
to demonstrate proof of concept, cells were printed in both inks to show that is possible.
Within the time scope of this thesis cell culture length was between 1-21 days. For C2C12
bioprints the used ﬂow rate was 204 nl/mm and the used robot speed was 3 mm/s. Muscle
strips were printed using a cell concentration of 10 mio. cells/ml and 20 mio. cells/ml,
respectively. After 2-3 days, muscle strips with no anchoring to ribbons turned into balls
within the wells. Several muscle strips for ﬁbrin ink with 10 mio. cells/ml lost their grip
to the ribbons and thereby lost their print path shape. Muscle strips with ﬁbrin ink for 20
mio. cells/ml held their shape and ribbon anchoring for longer (>7 days). Collagen printed
muscle strips with 10 mio. cells/ml showed cell spreading after several days (<7 days) even
though they held their compact structure the ﬁrst couple of days.
Across ink formulations and cell densities, we printed a total of 72 cell constructs, as seen
in Section 8.1 and Section 8.2, a total of 63 strips where found to have ribbon anchoring to
both ribbons on day 1. This gives a yield for our printing protocol of 87.5%. On day 14, a
total of 38 out of the 72 survived at maintained their shape. This gives us a yield of 52.8%.
The mean heights from the muscle strips with both collagen and ﬁbrin decrease over time
due to cell compaction.
The mean widths vary over time for both collagen and ﬁbrin muscle strips. Collagen length
compaction is in the interval of 17-23%, for ﬁbrin (10 mio. cells/ml) it is 25-28%, and for
ﬁbrin 20 mio. cells/ml it is 3-25%. The changes in width vary even more. Collagen strips
tend to degrade over time and hereby increase their width. Hence, the width compaction of
collagen strips day 14 compared to day 1 is from 29% smaller to 24% larger. For ﬁbrin 10
mio. cells/ml, it is 1-26% smaller to 6% larger. In ﬁbrin 20 mio. cells/ml the width decreases
65
==PAGE:86==
in the interval 1-19%.
For human cells, we have successfully printed the 1-strand, 4-strand, and 8-strand sizes in
ﬁbrin ink. The muscle strips have successfully maintained their shape after enzymatic slurry
removal. The strips have been not yet been further evaluated. Cells are still in culture and
we expect to see good anchoring to ribbons and nice myotube formation after 14-21 days in
culture.
It can be concluded that we successfully developed an embedded 3D printing protocol for
printing muscle tissue precursors with ﬁbrin-based and collagen-based ink into a DIW 3D-
developed device for muscle tissue culture. Fig. 41A) demonstrates the aim we had and B)
what we achieved. It has been shown that muscle strips can be developed based on enzymatic
cross-linking reactions as well as heat-sensitive cross-linking. Our protocol has demonstrated
a quick and easy way of controlling print path parameters and overcoming current challenges
within this ﬁeld of in vitro tissue models, like tissue casting. Engineering muscle tissue strips
fabricated with our protocol allows for easily and quickly changing muscle strip sizes, amount
of ECM components, cell density, slurry degradation time points, and slurry composition.
The eﬀectiveness of the design and the repeatability of printing the same muscle strip quickly
is another success for our protocol.
66
==PAGE:87==
Figure 41: A) Sketch of a desired muscle strip formation in the designed 8-well plate with
suspending ribbons for tissue anchoring. B) Our muscle strip formation using human skeletal
muscle cells in our fabricated 8-well device, showing tissue anchoring. This image is manipu-
lated and inserted to demonstrate the connection between our aim and the achieved muscle
strip.
67
==PAGE:88==
12
Future work
This thesis has investigated a lot of parameters, however there is still a lot of parameters
that has not been investigated, which could be investigated further in the future.
12.1
Device
For our 8-well device optimization features could include making the ribbons smaller. It was
not within the time scope of this thesis to fully optimize the printing protocol for the device,
however some parameters have been optimized along the way. Firstly, it is desired to change
the nozzle size for ribbons to achieve ribbons with a diameter of 100 µm instead. Printing
with a smaller nozzle would include changing the pressure and evaluating potential clogging
issue. Then the ribbon path should be changed to print all ribbons as one long trace to avoid
start/stop defects during ribbon printing. Additionally, the glass ink layer varies in height
from device to device, which requires manually changing the height of ribbons several times,
thus this layer could perhaps be optimized for more consistent evenness.
When keeping device in culture for longer periods of time (7-14 days) we did experience some
leaking issues from the wells. Since the device consists of many diﬀerent layers, materials and
parameters there are a lot of places within the device where failure might happen. Having
fewer aspects of the device could decrease the chances of failure. Leaking issues could also
be investigated over time by looking at if the failure happens the same place over time -
e.g. where the gasket layer starts and ends or if the layer should be larger. There is also a
variation in which person manually attaches the PS wells to the gasket layer.
12.2
Bioink and slurry formulations
The bioink formulations have remained constant throughout our thesis, however it could be
tested what perhaps increasing the ﬁbrin concentration might do, or using a diﬀerent kind
of collagen type for the collagen ink to evaluate stiﬀness and cell remodelling. Looking into
the slurry formulations, you could also try printing more in pure alginate or in pure cNFC.
We have demonstrated that pure alginate did not work as well as alginate with cNFC when
looking at compaction rates. In [19] C. Radeke used pure cNFC, which might give our muscle
strips too much compaction or less printing resolution. Increasing thrombin in slurry might
68
==PAGE:89==
also aﬀect the printed muscle strips for the ﬁbrin ink. As we saw cell spreading for collagen
ink before day 7, other ECM components, e.g., increasing Geltrex amount, could be added
to support the myoﬁber structure. Our ﬁbrin network seemed to hold its shape for up to
7-14 days, however adding ACA for longer periods might still be a good idea to help prolong
the integrity and durability of the ﬁbrin matrix for longer culture times (up to 4 weeks).
12.3
Using a 6-well plate
A future aspect could be to continue more work with printing our ribbons in a commercial
6 well plate. We have demonstrated that is is possible to print ribbons with the same height
of ⇠500 µm in a 6 well, however re-deﬁning the substrate and hitting the correct alignment
and height when printing muscle strips have to be optimized. Using a commercial 6 well
plate could be a faster method as it included fewer fabrication steps.
When increasing
the well diameter from Ø6 mm to Ø35 mm there is a need for larger amounts of support
gel. This means making alginate slurry and ﬁbers would require an upscale of the current
protocol. The device presented in [24] included 3D printed walls around the ribbons within
a 6 well plate, this could decrease the needed amount of slurry support, however it would
require several printing steps and a higher printing time. These walls would also need to be
examined for potential leaking issues.
12.4
Print path
The print path have been designed to evaluate three diﬀerent muscle strip sizes, however
other sizes could also be evaluated by either changing the layers (we had 4) or the strands
(we had 1, 4 and 8). We have also kept the needle size constant of printing with a 400
µm long needle. Evaluating print paths for other needle sizes could also be interesting, in
terms of shear during extrusion and how it could impact the cells. Having a smaller ﬁlament
size might have a potential impact on more accurate alignment. The theoretical diﬀusion
distance for oxygen diﬀusion in in vitro muscle tissue is ⇠100-150 µm [1]. When engineering
our 8-strand muscle strip with a height of 0.92 mm, we are far above thus value. However,
the muscle strips with 8-strand survived until day 14. Therefore, seeing how even larger
muscle strips would behave could be interesting. Engineered muscle tissue can have many
other applications than we aimed for, which could require muscle strips in cm scale. When
myoﬁbers are arranged in parallel, their contraction strength is maximized. If one desires
69
==PAGE:90==
tissue with stronger contractions, it would be an advantage to have larger strands, more
layers, and more strands. However, there will eventually be an upper limit for oxygen and
nutrient diﬀusion. Beyond this limit, there will be a risk of necrotic core formation, and the
strength of the tissue will decrease. Future work could also focus on deﬁning this limit.
12.5
Cells
It would have been ideal for this thesis to be able to work with cells from earlier on to be
able to evaluate the strips further. However, cross-linking issues did not allow for this. As
we know from literature cells do not grow the same in 3D as they do in 2D (when we culture
them), therefore inducing electrical stimulation by using our conductive ribbons would be
a future goal. Unfortunately, we had diﬃculty visualizing cells in the printed muscle strips
and, therefore, did not see good diﬀerentiation and myotube formation. Slicing muscle strips
or printing them in smaller constructs would be an idea for better staining and visualization
of cells. Working more with human muscle cells would also be a future goal, to create a
more physiologically relevant model in our platform. The human muscle tissue should be
evaluated further based on staining and markers for muscle maturation.
12.6
Clinical application
The protocol for developing muscle strips, with further advancement in electrical stimulation,
could ﬁnd clinical application in drug screening. Thanks to the size of both the tissue and
the wells in the 8-well device, the costs for ink and bioink ingredients and cells will be lower
compared to commercial well plates. Drug screening is relevant both for the development of
new therapeutic methods and in advancing the ﬁeld of personalized medicine. Implementing
the protocol is important as skeletal muscle diseases have a great aﬀect on patients life
quality.
70
==PAGE:91==

Appendix A
13.1
Protocol for thawing, splitting and freezing cells
74
==PAGE:95==
Protocol for Splitting & Thawing & Freezing C2C12 Cell Line
Chemicals:
●
Complete growth medium: DMEM HG (10 % FBS (foetal bovine serum), 1 % P/S)
●
Differentiation medium: DMEM HG (2 % HS (horse serum), 1 % P/S)
●
PBS (without Ca/Mg, D8537, Sigma)
●
Trypsin/EDTA
!!! Everything must be done with sterile technique in the hood. Before beginning,
1. Spray down the biosafety cabinet with virkon solution.
2. Wipe the biosafety cabinet with 70% ethanol.
3. Spray down the biosafety cabinet with 70% ethanol and wait until it evaporates.
4. From this point forward, spray off everything that goes into the hood with 70% ethanol.
Thawing cryopreserved cells:
1. Remove the cryovial containing the frozen cells from -150
οC storage, put into the dry ice
during transferring, and immediately place it into a 37°C water bath prepared with warm tap
water.
2. Hold the vial in the water bath without submerging the cap, and gently swirl the tube until
there is just a small bit of ice left in the vial. (It is important not to over-thaw the cryovials as
the presence of DMSO is toxic to the cells).
3. Transfer the vial into a laminar flow hood. Before opening, wipe the outside of the vial with
70% ethanol.
4. Transfer the desired amount of pre-warmed complete growth medium appropriate for your
cell line dropwise into the 15 mL centrifuge tube containing the thawed cells.
5. Centrifuge the cell suspension at approximately 800 rpm for 5 minutes.
6. After the centrifugation, check the clarity of the supernatant and visibility of a complete pellet.
Remove the supernatant without disturbing the cell pellet.
7. Gently resuspend the cells in the complete growth medium and transfer them into the
appropriate culture flask.
o
If you use the vial with high concentration of cells (5x10^6 cells in 1 mL), you can use
the cells directly for experimenting or seed them in T500 or 3xT175 flasks.
o
If you use the vial with low concentration of cells (5X10^5 cells in 1 mL), seed them in
T75 and expand until reaching the desired cell number based on the ratio in the table
below.
==PAGE:96==
8. Gently shuffle, ensure even dispersal, and return the fresh flask to the incubator. (Label plates
with the cell type, initials, date, passage number, and the date of the next splitting.)
Splitting cells:
1. Confirm that cells are around 50-70% confluent by microscopy.
2. 30-45 minutes before splitting cells, move the trypsin to thaw and pre-warm the complete
medium in the 37 °C metal bead bath.
3. Aspirate the old media from the 75 cm2 flask using a sterile autoclaved glass pipette. (Do not
touch the cells with the pipette.)
4. Careful not to disturb cells adhered to the wall with the flow; add 5 mL PBS.
5. Gently put D-PBS over cells to wash off the media by gently rocking it over the cells on the
flask wall.
6. Quickly aspirate D-PBS out. Cells will detach if D-PBS is left on them for too long.
7. Add 3 mL trypsin and gently rock the flask to cover cells. (Trypsin is harmful to the cells. Pay
special attention that cells are not in trypsin longer than 5 minutes.)
8. Incubate cells with trypsin at 37 °C for 2-5 mins (depending on cell line) until cells lift off.
9. During incubation, quickly prepare and label a 15 mL falcon tube for the next steps.
10. Remove cells from the incubator and quickly smack! Until cells are no longer attached to the
surface of the flask. (Tip the plate back and forth and ensure the cells have detached and are
running freely down the plate.)
11. Add 7mL warm complete medium, rinsing multiple times by pipetting up and down to
neutralise the trypsin reaction. Pipette towards the cell side of flask walls. Trypsin may destroy
your cells if you are too fast to neutralise it.
12. Transfer contents/cells to a 15 mL falcon tube. Spin down 800 rpm for 5 minutes at room
temperature. (Make sure the rotor is balanced!) While spinning, count with leftovers after
trypsinization, EtOH, and label new flasks, noting the +1 passage number and dilution.
13. Aspirate media from falcon tubes with cells; ensure not to disturb the pellet.
14. Add the calculated amount of media (according to the splitting ratio) to the pellet and pipette
up and down to homogenise.
***For the general culture of C2C12:
Split cells every second day as they duplicate fast. Do not need to count cells each time. You can use
around 1:5 dilution or surface area ratio. If you do not need cells, dilute cells to continue with the
==PAGE:97==
same flask size and trash or freeze the rest of cells (see freezing protocol below). For counting cells,
use the table below.
Eg., estimated cell numbers: from T75 (75 cm2) to 5xT75 OR 2xT175 OR 1xT500 (3-layer flask).
15. Expected amounts for maintaining cells:
TABLE2: Approximate cell numbers and media volumes for flasks that are adjusted to C2C12 cells
for expansion & maintenance of cells.
16.Gently shuffle, ensure even dispersal, and return the fresh flask to the incubator. (Label plates
with the cell type, initials, date, passage number, and the date of the next splitting.)
***For an experiment, count cells before seeding:
After resuspending the pellet into A mL, take 100-200 uL cell suspension into the Eppendorf tube
and count the cells using the counting cassette. Insert the cassette into the holder and run the
software to determine cell concentration (cells/mL). Measure viable cells as multiplying with viable
cell % shown on the counter’s software.
o
Calculation for preparing 107cells in 1 mL:
Measured cell concentration (cells/mL) ⋅a (mL) = 107 cells/mL * 1 mL
(a=amount that you need to take from A mL resuspension.)
Note1: Check your total cell number whether enough to make your desired cell number.
Note2: If you find a volume higher than desired volume, this means your main solution is too
diluted - you have limited cells. Therefore, you need to take the calculated amount and
re-centrifuge again. After centrifugation you can resuspend the pellet in desired volume to make
your sample solution.
TABLE2: Approximate cell numbers and media volumes for plates that are adjusted to C2C12 cells
for experimenting.
==PAGE:98==
15. Seed the remaining cells in growth media based on cell numbers and volumes below.
16. Gently shuffle, ensure even dispersal, and return the fresh flask to the incubator. (Label plates
with the cell type, initials, date, passage number, and the date of the next splitting.)
Important Notes
●
Seeding density for maintaining: 20 000 - 50 000 cells/ cm2 in flasks during maintenance
suggested. Over-confluency causes variations in differentiation of cells.
●
Seeding density for differentiations study: 50 000 cells/cm2 for 2D cell culture or 107
cells/mL for 3D cell culture studies
●
For differentiation:
-
2D cell culture: change medium after 24h incubation with differentiation medium (every
2-3 days, change media)
-
3D cell culture: change medium after 48h incubation with differentiation medium (every
2-3 days, change media)
Cryopreservation of cells:
Freezing medium: DMEM HG (10 % FBS, 1 % P/S) + 10% DMSO
1. Follow the splitting cells procedure until the step for collecting cell pellets.
2. Resuspend the cell pellet in growth media.
3. Define the volume of growth media based on 1 mL x cryovial number (5x10^6 cells/vial).
E.g., If you have 106 cells in total, you can freeze 20 vials. So, resuspend cells in 18 mL growth media.
After homogenising cells, add 2 mL DMSO and pipette up and down once and quickly transfer 1 mL
cell suspension into each cryovial.
4. Transfer cryovials to Mr. Frosty and keep them in the -80°C freezer overnight. Then,
transfer the vials to the -135°C freezer to the TMAT box (Rack: F; First box from the top).
==PAGE:99==
13.2
Protocol for preparing alginate gel
79
==PAGE:100==
Alginate slurry protocol
Protocol by: Gitte B. Hass and Therese R. Wassberg (March, 2024)
Alginate slurry protocol..................................................................................................... 1
Prepare 5% cNFC stock solution for alginate slurry........................................................7
●
Mix 1% w/v alginate (80-120 cP, Wako) solution in MilliQ water in a 100 mL glass
jar.
●
Take alginate from stock in Lab 005, and store in a speed mix container in the
fridge.
●
Stir the solution for 2 hours on the magnetic stirrer at 80°C.
●
Make sure that the magnet used has the same size as the 100 ml glass jar.
●
Set the temperature on both the stirrer and the probe.
●
Cover the water bath with tin foil.
1
==PAGE:101==
●
Prepare 37 g/L NaHCO3 solution in MilliQ water.
●
Adjust the pH of the solution to 9.5 using NaOH.
●
Store it at 4 °C.
●
Prepare a 2 mg/mL CaCO3 solution in MilliQ water in a sterile beaker.
●
Prepare a 1M and 2mM NaOH solution in MilliQ water in a sterile beaker.
●
After the 2 hours on the magnet stirrer mix 2 mg/mL CaCO3 solution (from the stock
above) 1:1 with the alginate solution and stir for 1 h at room temperature using the
magnetic stirrer.
●
Make sure that the magnet has the same size as the 100 ml glass jar.
●
The alginate solution will start gelling immediately.
●
Add acetic acid 1:500 ratio (200 ul in 100 ml) and leave on the magnet stirrer for 4
hours at 650 rpm - still at room temperature.
2
==PAGE:102==
●
After 2 hours the solution will appear viscous.
●
The gelled alginate solution should be mechanically fragmented into microparticles
by homogenizing it at 15.000 rpm for 10 min with a T25 Ultra-Turrax homogenizer
(IKA) equipped with S25N-10G dispersing tool.
3
==PAGE:103==
●
Divide the solution evenly into sterile 50 ml falcon tubes (important that it is even for
centrifuging).
●
Centrifuge the falcon tubes at 18.500 G for 20 minutes in the ultra speed centrifuge
with fiber rotor (in lab 005)
4
==PAGE:104==
●
After centrifuging discard the supernatant - use a pipette to take it out (don’t pour
from the falcon tubes as gel at the bottom might fall out)
●
Incubate overnight in DMEM + 2 mM NaOH + 1% P/S (1 part slurry, 2 parts DMEM)
5
==PAGE:105==
●
Adjust the pH by adding 2 mM NaOH dropwise. Color should change from yellow to
orange/red color.
●
The pH should be 7 - take out small amount with pipette and test on pH strip.
●
Store in the fridge until further use.
●
Before using alginate for bioprinting the particles are homogenized at 15.000 rpm
for 3 minutes with a T25 Ultra-Turrax homogenizer (IKA) equipped with S25N-10G
dispersing tool and centrifuged at 18.500 G for 10 min in the ultra speed centrifuge
with fiber rotor (in lab 005) - add 0,5% chloroform to the solution before centrifuging
to make solution sterile.
6
==PAGE:106==
●
After centrifuging discard the supernatant - use a pipette to suck it out (don’t pour
from falcon tubes as gel at the bottom might fall out). The pellet will be quite visible
so don’t shake falcon tube around.
●
The pellet of jam-packed alginate microparticles can be stored in the fridge at 4 °C
until further use.
Prepare 5% cNFC stock solution for alginate slurry
●
Weigh the cNFC20 8x fibers on ionized weight at the lab 213 second floor.
7
==PAGE:107==
●
1 g of cNFC20 8x fiber is for 20 ml DMEM + 1% P/S
●
Add calculated media amount to the fibers in a falcon tube and press down the
fibers so everything can soak in the media.
●
Leave to soak overnight in the fridge
●
The day after homegenize the pre-soake cNFC solution in the LAF bench at 10.000
rpm for 5 minutes with a T25 Ultra-Turrax homogenizer (IKA) equipped with
S25N-10G dispersing tool - the solution should be clear without any aggregates.
8
==PAGE:108==
●
If there are a lot of bubbles in the mixture, centrifuge it at 2500 rpm for 2 minutes.
9
==PAGE:109==
13.3
Protocol for making the 8 well ribbon device
89
==PAGE:110==
8 well ribbon platform protocol
Protocol by: Gitte B. Hass, Therese R. Wassberg and Jonathan B. Fjendsbo (March 2024)
Indholdsfortegnelse
8 well ribbon platform protocol...................................................................................................................... 1
Preparation in advance................................................................................................................................1
Cleaning and preparing SEBS..............................................................................................................1
Vacuum forming 8 wells and lids...........................................................................................................1
Day 1 - preparations of substrate................................................................................................................ 3
Day 2 - ink preparations and printing...........................................................................................................4
Glass ink............................................................................................................................................... 4
Graphite ink...........................................................................................................................................4
Gasket ink.............................................................................................................................................5
Printing procedure.................................................................................................................................5
Day 2 - ready to use.................................................................................................................................... 6
Preparation in advance
●
Before printing day you will need the 8 wells, lids for the 8 wells and prepared
substrate slides.
Cleaning and preparing SEBS
●
See separate guide for this.
Vacuum forming 8 wells and lids
●
Vacuum form the 8 wells and the lids using the metal molds. (one mold for lids and
one mold for 8 wells)
1
==PAGE:111==
●
Use the vacuum former in building 345.
●
Turn on the vacuum former on the back left side.
●
Place the polystyrene sheet (thickness: 2 mm) in the vacuum former and pull it up.
●
Place metal mold at the center on the bottom plate of the vacuum former.
●
Vacuum former settings: HIPS, 180 degrees, K 2.00, V 0.
2
==PAGE:112==
●
Temperature increase will be shown in the big field on the screen.
●
Keep an eye out, once it hits 180 degrees the vacuum will start, and you have to
pull down the sheet.
●
Pull down at a speedy pace, but not too fast.
●
It is important to pull it all the way down (until you feel like you overcome a
notch/click)
●
Leave the sheets to cool off for a while.
●
The sheet on the mold for lids can be pressed out, by pressing all over the plate for
several minutes until it loosens.
●
The sheet from the 8 wells will have to be laser cut before they can be removed.
●
Use the file XXXXX.xxx to laser cut them
●
In order to get the holes for the 8 wells, each well plate will have to be sanded (with
sandpaper).
Day 1 - preparations of substrate
●
Turn on the printer.
●
Prepare biosurfine. Do not leave the Biosurfine in daylight for longer than necessary
as it is very sensitive to UV light.
●
The mixing ratio is: 1:1:1,5 (Biosurfine: Water (MilliQ): Ethanol (96%))
3
==PAGE:113==
●
Add everything to an eppendorf tube and pipette up and down to mix.
●
Store it in the fridge in a light sealed bag (or aluminium foil).
●
While storing the biosurfine prepare the substrate:
●
The polystyrene substrate is UV ozone treated for 10 minutes. This is done right
before use (within the same three hours) as this greatly affects the spreading of the
ink.
●
Carry the slides in a closed container.
●
Load the biosurfine into a 1 ml Hamilton syringe.
●
Place substrate holder and UV treated substrate in the printer.
●
Perform surface height measurement and needle length measurement before
printing.
●
Print the biosurfine on the substrate using the XXX.xx file
●
Printer settings: printhead = 2, Initial Height 2 μm, needle; 60 μm (green), flow-rate
0.0050 ul/sec, push-retract disabled (0.00).
●
Whenever the substrate is replaced, surface height measurement must be
performed again.
●
After printing the Biosurfine is cured in a UV cure box on the second floor for 20
minutes at room temperature.
●
Store slides in a box for printing the next day, or print the rest of the device right
away.
Day 2 - ink preparations and printing
Glass ink
●
The glass spheres you need:
●
In a speedmix container mix SEBS cleaned with Soxhlet extraction in ButAc (33%
w/w) with 75% w/w silanized glass spheres (add silanized glass spheres to the
SEBS/ButAc 33% w/w solution
NOTE: A 75% w/w solution corresponds to adding equal amounts (by weight) of the
33% SEBS solution and the silanized glass spheres
●
Wrap the cup with parafilm.
●
Speed mix the ink at 2500 rpm for 5 minutes in a speed mixer (WITHOUT a mixing
ball!)
4
==PAGE:114==
●
The ink should look like this:
●
Load the ink into a 5 cc cartridge. Scoop it up using a clean spatula and press it to
the end with the white cap for the cartridge.
●
Printing settings: printhead = 4; speed = 10 mm/s, SSD = 350, P = roughly 150 kPa,
initial height = 510 μm, nozzle size = 510 μm plastic (pink)
Graphite ink
●
The graphite particles you need:
●
In a speed mixer cup mix SEBS cleaned with Soxhlet extraction in ButAc (37% w/w)
with 20% w/w graphite 1 µm (add graphite to the SEBS/ButAc 37% w/w solution.
●
Do NOT wrap this cup with parafilm!
●
Speed mix the ink at 2500 rpm for 5 minutes in a speed mixer (WITH a mixing ball!)
●
The ink should look like this:
5
==PAGE:115==
●
Load the ink into a 3 cc cartridge. Scoop it up using a clean spatula and press it to
the end with the white cap for the cartridge.
●
Clean the mixing ball with acetone and water - it can be used again.
●
Printing settings: printhead = 3, P = roughly 400 kPa, 200 μm plastic (clear)
Gasket ink
●
Use the SEBS cleaned with Soxhlet extraction in ButAc (37% w/w).
●
Load the SEBS into a 3 cc cartridge using a clean spatula.
●
Centrifuge the cartridge for 2 minutes at 2500 rpm to remove the air bubbles.
NOTE: remember to weigh the cartridge to find correct counterweight.
●
Printing settings: printhead = 1, P = roughly 120 kPa, 840 μm plastic (Green)
Printing procedure
●
Load all the cartridges into the correct printheads.
●
Place substrate holder in the printer like this:
NOTE: remember to add the metal rods in the corresponding holes.
●
Place the substrate holder in the holder, remember to have the side with the
biosurfine facing upwards and to the right side in the holder.
●
Do needle length measurement for all needles.
●
Do surface height measurement each time you use a new substrate.
6
==PAGE:116==
●
Run the file XXX.xx. The file will first print the glass ink, then the ribbons and then
the gasket ink.
●
Once the gasket ink is printed take the substrate out and press the vacuum formed
8 wells on top. Apply a light pressure for around 10 seconds.
NOTE: do not press too lightly as the device won't seal then and do not press too
firmly as the ribbons might break.
●
The finished device should be left in the fume hood overnight for ButAc to
evaporate.
Day 2 - ready to use
●
After being left out overnight the devices can be stored in a box until further use or
they can be cleaned for using directly.
7
==PAGE:117==
13.4
Protocol for cleaning SEBS
97
==PAGE:118==
Cleaning SEBS with soxhlet extraction
Protocol by: Gitte B. Hass and Therese R. Wassberg (March, 2024)
Indholdsfortegnelse
Cleaning SEBS with soxhlet extraction.........................................................................................................1
Day 1: extraction..........................................................................................................................................1
Day 2: Remove SEBS and dry.................................................................................................................... 4
Day 3: Mix with butylacetat..........................................................................................................................7
Day 4-5: Stir.................................................................................................................................................7
Day 6: Ready to use....................................................................................................................................7
Day 1: extraction
●
Measure out 200 ml ethyl acetate in measuring flask.
●
Add 200 ml ethyl acetate to a round flask in Lab 223 on the second floor.
●
Take out the setup:
1
==PAGE:119==
●
The setup should include:
●
Add the 200 ml ethyl acetate to a round flask using a cork holder.
2
==PAGE:120==
●
Add SEBS to the filter:
●
Add the filter with SEBS as shown:
●
Place the setup as this:
3
==PAGE:121==
●
Connect the water hose and the power.
●
Put on setting 6.
●
Remember to close the hood and leave a sign with your name and when you will
pick up.
●
Wait 1 hour until it bubbles.
●
Leave it overnight - do not set the setting higher than 6!
Day 2: Remove SEBS and dry
●
The next day turn off the setup and collect the SEBS.
●
Clean a glass petri dish with acetone.
●
Collect the SEBS using a clean spatula - stick it into the mass and lift SEBS + filter
out of glass wear.
4
==PAGE:122==
●
Scrape out the SEBS and place into the clean petri dish.
●
It will come out in lumps, and it fill feel sticky and firm
●
Chop it up with the spatula into smaller pieces:
5
==PAGE:123==
●
Place the petri dish in the oven at 60°C and leave overnight
6
==PAGE:124==
Day 3: Mix with butylacetat
●
Remove SEBS from the oven.
●
Turn the degrees down to 0°C and let air in with the air valve
●
Take out the petri dish
●
Add the SEBS to a blue cap bottle while weighing it
●
Add desired amount of butylacetat for either 33% or 37,5% stock solution in grams
by using a disposable pipette
●
Label the blue cap bottle and wrap it with parafilm.
●
Store in the fume cabinet.
Day 4-5: Stir
●
Stir the solution for the following days until the mixture is homogeneous.
7
==PAGE:125==
Day 6: Ready to use
8

13.5
Protocol for doing embedded 3D print of C2C12 muscle cells
in alginate slurry
106
==PAGE:127==
Embedded 3D printing with C2C12 cells
Protocol by Gitte B. Hass and Therese R. Wassberg (March, 2024)
Indholdsfortegnelse
Embedded 3D printing with C2C12 cells....................................................................................................... 1
Preparations the day before:....................................................................................................................... 1
Preparations on bioprinting day:..................................................................................................................2
Cell preparation on bioprinting day:.............................................................................................................5
Ink preparation on bioprinting day:.............................................................................................................. 6
What to do with embedded prints on bioprinting day:................................................................................. 8
What to do with embedded prints the day after bioprinting day:................................................................. 8
Preparations the day before:
●
Gather everything you need for the next day. (ribbon platform, glass syringe 1 ml,
syringe adapter, speed mixer containers (1 for each type of slurry and 1 for each
type of ink), blue nozzle, blue needle (400 um))
●
In LAF bench spray everything with 70% ethanol EXCEPT the ribbon platforms and
leave to air dry (remember the flow must be on in the bench)
●
Air gun the ribbon platforms to get rid of dust.
●
Once everything is dry and clean put in a clean box and close with a clean lid (Store
it for the next day)
●
Put pipette box in the fridge (both positive displacement and regular)
●
Make sure you SHAPER design is ready before printing day.
●
Check that alginate slurry is ready (adjust pH to 7-7.4 if it is not already)
-
If it is not adjusted:
o
In a LAF bench resuspend the alginate particles in twice the amount of
growth medium (DMEM + 10 % FBS + 1 % P/S)
o
Incubate overnight at room temperature.
o
Centrifuge the suspension at 18.500 g for 10 min and carefully discard the
supernatant (you can use pipettes for this)
-
If it is adjusted:
o
In a LAF bench make the slurry composition you need in a sterile speed
mixer cup that has been under UV for at least 20 min.
NOTE: if material is very viscous use positive displacement pipette. If material is
very liquid use the regular pipettes.
a. Slurry for fibrin ink:
●
4.5 ml in total using:
o
3 ml Alginate
o
150 ul thrombin (1000 u/mL)
o
600 ul DMEM + 1% P/S
o
750 ul cNFC (from 5% stock)
1
==PAGE:128==
●
Mix everything EXCEPT the thrombin (this must only be added
on the day of use)
●
Stir the mixture around with the positive displacement pipette
tip and pipette a few times up and down.
b. Slurry for collagen ink (2:1 ratio of slurry to cNFC):
●
4.5 ml in total using:
o
3 ml Alginate
o
1,5 ml cNFC
●
Stir the mixture around with the positive displacement pipette
tip and pipette a few times up and down.
c. Seal the cups with parafilm and store in the fridge for the next day.
Preparations on bioprinting day:
●
Put everything inside LAF bench. Remember to open all the lids and place them
with insides facing out.
●
Sterilize everything using UV light for at least 20 min.
●
Turn on the cooler (placed to the left under the bioprinter (check that switch in the
back is set at 2))
●
Set the temperature at Tset at 5°C (it must never be below 0°C!) - and press start at
the lower right corner on the touch screen.
NOTE: sometimes it must be pressed twice - the machine will make noise when its running,
so you will know when it has started
2
==PAGE:129==
●
Turn on printer software program and activate printhead to start the cooling process
(do this at least 1 hour before printing)
●
Clean printer surfaces with 70% ethanol (wipe with dust free tissue) - remember to
turn on the flow in the bench.
●
Gather all the needed materials and make sure to keep it ON ICE!:
o
HEPES
o
NaHCO3
o
Xanthan Gum (1%)
o
DMEM + 1% P/S
o
Collagen 1 (10 mg/ml)
o
Geltrex (8-11 mg/ml)
o
Alginate slurry (+cNFC) that was made the day before.
●
Finish making slurry compositions.
o
On ice add the thrombin to the slurry used for fibrin ink
o
Stir the mixture around with the positive displacement pipette tip and pipette
a few times up and down.
●
Speed mix the slurry containers for 30 seconds at 3000 rpm in the speed mixer
3
==PAGE:130==
●
Store finished containers in the fridge.
●
The ink and slurry should look like this:
●
In each well of the 8-well device add 150 ul of slurry with positive displacement
pipette.
●
DO NOT press the tip onto the ribbons when adding.
4
==PAGE:131==
●
Add slurry to all the devices. Place a sterile lid on top. Place in a sterile petri dish
and place in the fridge.
●
This way the devices are ready once ink is ready.
Cell preparation on bioprinting day:
●
DO NOT thaw cells before needing them!
●
ONLY thaw them right before adding to the ink formulation
●
Use cells from frozen stock.
●
Thaw cells in a warm water bath and stir until liquid is formed around an ice block in
the vial.
●
Ad drops of warm DMEM + 1% P/S little by little to the vial.
●
Suck up everything in a pipette and add to a falcon tube prefilled with 15 mL warm
DMEM + 1% P/S.
●
Pipette up and down to ensure an even mix of cells in the media.
●
Take out 100 ul and add to an Eppendorf tube for cell counting.
●
Centrifuge the falcon tube at 800 rpm for 5 minutes - remember a counterweight.
5
==PAGE:132==
Ink preparation on bioprinting day:
●
Prepare the inks on the ice.
NOTE: if material is very viscous use positive displacement pipette. If material is very
liquid use the regular pipettes.
o
Ink with fibrin: 1 ml in total. Mix in the order as listed.
▪
166 ul Matrigel
▪
134 ul Fibrin
▪
250 ul Xanthan Gum
▪
Cells: 450 ul warm DMEM + 1% P/S for 10 mio. Cells/mL
o
CAREFULLY pipette mixture up and down - DO NOT overstrain the cells.
o
Ink with collagen: 1.1 ml in total. Mix in the order as listed.
▪
150 ul NaHCO3
▪
150 ul HEPES
▪
250 ul Xanthan Gum
▪
300 ul Collagen-1 (3 mg/ml)
●
Pipette collagen just before adding the cells. Once neutralized
it polymerizes extremely fast!
▪
150 ul Geltrex (%15)
●
Mix Geltrex with cell pellet and warm DMEM + 1% P/S
▪
Cells: 100 ul 10 mio. Cells/ml in total. XX ul pellet + XX ul DMEM +
1% P/S.
o
CAREFULLY pipette mixture up and down - DO NOT overstrain the cells.
o
Speed mix both ink containers in a speed mixer for 5 seconds at 3000 rpm.
●
Load pure alginate into a syringe to fill dead volume with a sterile blue nozzle.
●
Pull pure alginate all the way down when holding the needle perpendicular.
●
Press all the way up (without extruding in out) to get rid of air bubbles.
6
==PAGE:133==
(shown with beads)
●
Load ink with the same nozzle. Avoid getting air into it when loading.
●
Replace nozzle with sterile blue needle.
●
Place the syringe with the adapter into the pre-cooled printhead.
●
Start printing (see printing guide “R-gen100 printing guide”)
●
After printing one device:
o
Place the lid on top.
o
Add to a sterile petri dish.
o
Add PBS or sterile water around the device in the petri dish.
o
Place in the small incubator in the bioprinting lab.
7
==PAGE:134==
What to do with embedded prints on bioprinting day:
●
After all the devices are printed:
o
Take the petri dishes from the small incubator and move to cell lab incubator.
o
Remember to label all the devices and petri dishes with permanent marker.
o
Leave devices in the incubator for at least 80 minutes to allow crosslinking to
happen.
o
After 80 minutes (it can be up to 2 hours) add 150 ul growth medium to each
well - add carefully drop by drop.
o
Place back in the incubator and leave until the next day.
What to do with embedded prints the day after bioprinting day:
●
The growth media should be replaced with differentiation media + enzymes (for
slurry degradation)
●
For alginate slurry + cNFC fibers mix:
o
Differentiation media (DMEM + 2% Horse Serum + 1% P/S) + 0.01 U/ml
Alginate Lyase (A1603-100MG 10.000 units/gram solid from
SIGMA-ALDRICH, CAS-no. 9024-15-1) + 5 U/ml Cellulase (Cellulase from
Aspergillus niger from Tokyo Chemical Industry Co. LTD, Cas: 9012-54-8)
NOTE: it is a good idea to make the above calculations in advance and have
the main stock in the fridge ready for use the next day
●
Make a stock of Alginate Lyase from 10K U/g to 1U/mL then in PBS, then filter it
with 0.45 um filter. Add 1% v/v enzyme stock to differentiation media (DM).
●
Make a stock of Cellulase from 17K U/g to 500 U/mL then in PBS, then filter it with
0.45 um filter. Add 1% v/v enzyme stock to differentiation media (DM).
●
Empty out old media and refresh with new differentiation media every two days
(without enzymes)
8
==PAGE:135==
9
==PAGE:136==

R-gen 100 printer guide

==PAGE:138==

==PAGE:141==

13.7
Protocol for Human Primary Skeletal Muscle Cells
124
==PAGE:145==
Bioprinting Protocol for Human Primary Skeletal Muscle Cells (HSkM)
Pre-Bioprinting: Media, Slurry and Cell Preparation
●
Slurry Preparation:
○
F10 Media: Submerge alginate slurry in F10 media for conditioning and ensure the final
pH is 7.(Ham's F-10 Nutrient Mix (thermofisher.com) - 11550 - Ham's F-10 Nutrient Mix |
Thermo Fisher Scientific - DK)
●
Media Preparation:
○
Cook Growth Media + 5 mg/mL Aminocaproic acid (ACA): For initial cell culture and
post-printing recovery.
■
Cook Growth Media: MyoTonic Basal Media (MB-2222) + MyoTonic
Growth Supplement (MS-3333) + Penicillin/Streptomycin (P/S): Media |
MyoTonic and CryoTonic | Cook MyoSite
■
6-Aminocaproic acid (ACA): 6-Aminocaproic acid = 99 titration, powder
60-32-2 (sigmaaldrich.com)
○
Curibio Differentiation Media (DM) and Maintenance Media (MM): For myotube
differentiation and maintenance (3D Skeletal Muscle Tissue Media — Curi Bio).
■
Curibio
Differentiation
(Diff)
Media
(0-7
days):
Add
1%
P/S,
Supplement A (specific to DM kit), and Supplement B
■
Curibio Maintenance
Media (+7 days): Add 1% P/S, Supplement A
(specific to MM kit), and Supplement B
●
Cell Preparation:
○
Thaw Cook Myocytes in Cook Growth Media.
○
Prepare a cell suspension at 10 million cells/mL in F10 media for fibrin-based bioink.
Bioink Preparation:
●
Bioink (Fibrin-based):
○
For 750 uL Bioink:
■
Geltrex: 124.5 µL (20%)
■
Cells: 450 µL in F10 media (7.5 million cells total)
■
Fibrinogen (50 mg/mL): 108 µL (10%)
■
Xanthan Gum: 187.5 µL (25%)
Support Bath Preparation:
●
For 2:1 dilution with Thrombin (4.5 mL):
○
Alginate Slurry: 3 mL
○
NFC + Thrombin Solution: 1.5 mL
■
Thrombin (100 U/mL): 150 µL (final concentration 10 U/mL in solution)
■
F10 media: 600 µL
■
NFC (5% stock): 750 µL (final concentration 2.5% in solution)
Post-Printing:
==PAGE:146==
1.
Immediate Post-Printing:
○
Crosslink the constructs for at least 80 minutes (up to 2 hours).
○
Carefully add 150 µL of F10 media dropwise to each well and incubate for 10 minutes.
○
Replace F10 media with Cook Growth Media + 5 ng/mL ACA and incubate for 24 hours.
2.
Culture and Differentiation:
○
Follow the same differentiation protocol as before:
■
24 hours (try not to over 24h) after incubating: Replace the Growth Media with
Curibio Diff media (For 0-7 days).
■
Refresh Curibio Diff media every 2-3 days for 7 days.
■
Switch to Curibio Maintenance media after 7 days and refresh every 2-3 days.
Important Considerations:
●
F10 media is a serum-free basal medium used temporarily during crosslinking and initial
incubation. Switch to growth media promptly to provide essential nutrients.
●
Avoid overculturing (not more than 24 hours) cells in growth media.
●
Curibio Diff and Maintenance media already contain ACA, so no additional supplementation
is needed.

==PAGE:197==
16
Appendix D
This Appendix includes results of initial experiments and cross-linking issue experiments.
16.1
SHAPER
The ﬁrst design we managed to try in SHAPER was made from drawing volumetric cylinders
on top of each other as seen in Fig. 42. This way meant each cylinder had to be placed
by changing the x, y and z-coordinates manually. The way of controlling parameters in this
way was diﬃcult and time consuming, since adding an extra layer would involve going back
to SHAPER and drawing another cylinder and deﬁning the coordinates again. Additionally,
this meant we had to deﬁne the parameters again, such as material type and ﬂow corrections.
After this the ﬁle had to be exported as a .bioﬁle and uploaded to the printer software (R-
GEN CONTROLLER).
Figure 42: Initial cylinder design in SHAPER.
Later in the process, we became acquainted with a new method of drawing by using the
planar function. This involved drawing lines, giving them a height and width (0.320 mm
x 5000 mm). By using the inbuilt "stacking" function i SHAPER, the lines was able to
be easily replicated in the exact same dimensions. Thus, the same sized muscle strip could
177
==PAGE:198==
be drawn much easier. The new design can be seen in Fig. 43. SHAPER has a clear ad-
vantage in its way of controlling substrate type and number of well. SHAPER was used to
deﬁne the 8 well device (substrate) and chose sequential printing path. The substrates ge-
ometry and placement within the substrate holder is deﬁned in SHAPER. Halfway through
our project the printer software got an update, which provided us with new possibilities of
quickly redeﬁning our print path and easy control of print parameters. The new software
called Pangolin could be provided with either a .bioﬁle or a .nc ﬁle. One great feature was
that it could convert our very long code from R-gen 100 controller (old software) to a .nc
ﬁle. The print path, ﬂow rate/ﬂow corrections, number of layers, number of passes, robot
speed and much more was then controlled directly in the .nc ﬁle by using G-code functions.
The G-codes for our diﬀerent experiments can be seen in App. 14. Several conditions have
been tested throughout our experiments, such as diﬀerent ﬂow corrections, ﬂow rates, print
paths, delays/no delays etc. The diﬀerent layouts for the muscle strip and following results
will be discussed later.
Figure 43: Initial planar design in SHAPER.
16.2
Muscle strip print path
Since we have the desired support gel condition and the desired in formulation we are testing
diﬀerent print paths to achieve the desired muscle strip size. As mentioned earlier embedded
3D printing of muscle strips have not been investigated thoroughly before in the literature.
This means the optimal size of the muscle strip is not known, that why we are testing both
178
==PAGE:199==
relatively small sizes and sizes more close to state of the art. We know that it can not be
to thin where there is a risk of breakage and it cannot be too large as we might have a
too necrotic core. The big advantage of having a smaller muscle strip is we can be more
repetitive in terms of future therapeutic testing strategies and that printing time will be less.
When testing ink solution content earlier the SHAPER software used to control the path,
therefore previuos experiments were just to determine the optimal conditions, but not so
much the muscle strip size. This led to some issues and diﬃculties as the .bioﬁle code was
500+ lines just for simple designs and the ﬁle followed its one language from SHAPER.
Since a software update in April 2024 the program is now Pangolin and here the .bioﬁle
from SHAPER (which has control of the substrate dimensions) can be exported as a .cn ﬁle,
where c-gode can be altered much easier. This has given us way more control over our design
and it is now possible to change more parameters way easier and speed up the process of
printing with several ﬂow rates, directions, passes etc.
Before printing the muscle strips it is tested what the spacing in µm size between the ribbons
will do for the print. This spacing needs to be adjusted before choosing a ﬁnal design to
make sure the muscle strip will be uniform without slurry in between the layers. Therefore
we tested what the path would look like with diﬀerent spacing. We tried overshooting with
700 µm from center between three passes in one layer, then with 350 µm from center to
center between ﬁve passes in one layer and then lastly 200 µm from center to center between
8 passes. The results can be seen in Fig. 44.
Figure 44: A) Three passes. B) ﬁve passes. C) 8 passes. Nozzle size 400 µm and ﬂow rate
102 nl/mm for all. Scale bar 500 µm
179
==PAGE:200==
It is clear to see that the spacing from center to center between passes has to be smaller
than the inner diameter of the needle (400 µm) in order for the layers to merge into a solid
muscle strip. It is therefore selected that the size is chosen to be 200 µm between centers
and between layers in the z-direction. Since the z-direction can be visualized as well, we
take the safe choice of making this 200 µm as well. As the next step we have tested what
we call for a small, medium and large muscle strip - all under three diﬀerent ﬂow rates and
three motor speeds. This experiment was tested along side an experiment to see what ﬂow
correction 0-5 means for extrusion of our material.
The results for diﬀerent ﬂow rates/ﬂow corrections can be seen in Fig. 45. We can see that
the higher the ﬂow correction (which was the setting from the .bioﬁle) the more material
comes out and the wider the strip will be. This ﬂow correction is transferred to the new
software with to a .cn, which means the ﬂowrate will increase by the number the ﬂow cor-
rection is sat to. So ﬂow correction 2 for a ﬂow rate of 102 nl/mm, will mean a ﬂow rate of
204 nl/mm and so forth. This will be able to give some control over the size of the muscle
strip.

(b)
(c)
(d)
(e)

correction 3 (306 nl/mm). D) Flow correction 4 (408 nl/mm) E) Flow correction 5 (500
nl/mm). Nozzle size 400µm for all. Scale bar 500µm.

ruined strips look like can be seen in Fig. 51.
Figure 51: Cross linking issues demonstrated for a 4 strand 4 layers strip. Constructs loose
their shape after slurry removal and sometimes by just adding media.

removal of the slurry on day 1 (the print day), day 2 and day 3, along with adding GM to
ensure cell survival. This experiment was done with C2C12 cells and followed the plan seen
in Tab. 10.

16.8
Cross-linking issues
Since none of the prints held their compact print path structure, we began looking in to the
cross linking between ﬁbrinogen and thrombin. Therefore, we ran an experiment where we
mixed diﬀerent concentrations of thrombin with the ﬁbrin ink (without cells and media, and
instead PBS). From Fig. 53 we can see that we tested a total of 14 tubes, where 2 tubes
were the control tubes, to have a fair comparison over time. We know that the control tubes
will not become a gel as they contain no thrombin, so ﬁbrin will not cross link. All tubes
where placed at 37 degrees celcius in an incubator. Each tube where looked at every 10-20
minute for a period of 2 hours. From this no geliﬁcation could be seen in either of the tubes.
We choose the conditions based of Khodabukus [2], where their muscle strip has ⇠0.5 U
187
==PAGE:208==
thrombin per mg of ﬁbrinogen. Therefore, we tested from this value and above. We know
our thrombin have to diﬀuse through slurry to reach the ﬁbrinogen in the ink, so we expect
that we need to use higher U. We left the tubes in the incubator over night to see the eﬀect.
However, still neither of the inks showed geliﬁcation.
Figure 53: The eppendorf tubes used to test diﬀerent thrombin concentrations to see cross
linking.
Since the cross linking was not optimal for either of the tubes, or the conditions we evaluated
it under weren’t optimal, we decided that while the eppendorf tubes experiment was running
for the next day as well, we might as well incubate an 8-well print as well. We kept the
Xhantan Gum in the ink, since it did not appear to be the one causing the problem. However
we also increased the thrombin concentration ﬁve times in the slurry (meaning we left out
the 600 µl DMEM) and had 750 µl thrombin in total instead. Then we also looked into not
giving the 8 well plates any media on the ﬁrst day, since adding media might also aﬀect the
prints. This can be due to thrombin going to low concentrations in the media and not the
low concentration in the printed construct. We made a new print with ﬁve times at much
thrombin and followed the plan in Tab. 11.
Table 11: Degradation time points
Plate 1
Plate 2
Day 1 (2 hours after print)

GM + enzymes

==PAGE:209==
is added on day 1 (the print day), the print will compact more until day 2 than previously
seen. However, when adding enzymes on day 2 the print spreads out - not at the same ratio
as the other ones, but still way too much. When adding enzymes 2 hours after printing, the
print path seems to break.
Figure 54: A) 2 hours after print (plate 1).

We did another experiment simultaneously, on two plates. where we allowed for thrombin
diﬀusion in alginate slurry for 7 days, while adding media with thrombin and media without
thrombin at day 2, day 4 and day 6 for a 4 strand print. On day 7 enzymes to degrade slurry
was intended to be added, however all the prints demonstrated far too wide of a spread and
the experiment was terminated. The conditions can be seen in Tab. 12

Results from this can be seen in Fig. 55 and Fig. 56, it is clear to see that thrombin did not
have an aﬀect on the compression of the print compared to the print that got regular media.
Additionally, it can be seen that even though no enzymes where added to the media, the
slurry degraded over time or got diluted, which also aﬀected the spreading of the prints. It
is relevant to take this into consideration, when determining slurry degradation time point
for cells, as cells need new fresh media everyday.

It is clear to see from both Fig. 57 and Fig. 58 that once again, the strips loose their shape
after slurry degradation. With this new stock of ﬁbrinogen and thrombin, the prints do not
spread out as much after degradation. However, the compactness is still not at all what we
are aiming for. We see that adding media 2 hours after printing does not aﬀect the prints
signiﬁcantly, which is good as when printing with cells, we would like to add media as soon
as possible.
Simultaneously, we were testing how the behavior of the 1 strand, 4 strand and 8 strand
designs would look like by following the plan seen in Tab. 14. We wanted to test if the
polymerization has happened at the same time (on day 2) for all bead strip sizes, before
adding media and later starting slurry degradation.

Figure 59: Printed 1 strand muscle strip following Tab. 14 (Plate 1). It is clear that the
print is ruined over time. Scale bar 500µm.A row only.
195
==PAGE:216==
Figure 60: Printed 4 strand muscle strip following Tab. 14 (Plate 2). It is clear that the

196
==PAGE:217==
Figure 61: Printed 8 strand muscle strip following Tab. 14 (Plate 3). It is clear that the
print is ruined over time. Scale bar 500µm. A row only.
The ﬁrst thing we notice from the images from 2 hours after print is that the 8 strand in
particular, has a very uneven strip formation - it looks very muscle stripy. It is believed that
since the new stock of ﬁbrinogen and thrombin already geliﬁes upon touch (ss seen in Fig.
62 earlier) the 8 strand design is gelling upon print, since the print speed for this is longer.
Therefore, the needle pulls in a mere gel-like material than previously seen for the 8 strand
design in Fig. 48, where thrombin and ﬁbrinogen did not cross link.
When looking at the designs after slurry degradation on day 4 it is clear that the strips
do not keep their full compaction as seen 2 hours after print or the following day, but that
spreading does happen. However, it looks better than previously seen and it is actually
possible to distinguish between what would be the 1, 4 or 8 strand, respectively.
197
==PAGE:218==
It is very clear that from our ﬁndings the main issue is gel formation. We have upped the
concentration of thrombin, which did not solve anything once removing slurry. We have
added thrombin to media to allow for diﬀusion over longer periods once removing old me-
dia. This did not have an aﬀect either and constructs still failed after slurry removal. We
have tried longer periods of cross-linking times before doing anything, this did not have an
aﬀect either and slurry degradation still ruined the constructs. As seen from the results so
far, it appears that polymerization is the main issue and that our constructs are unevenly
cross-linked resulting in fragile structures that break upon slurry removal, most likely due
to poor interconnect withing the gel.
We tested our stock of ﬁbrinogen and thrombin and evaluate the geliﬁcation by following the
Curi Bio testing guide for thrombin and ﬁbrinogen. This can be seen in App. ??. Following
the guide we mixed 137 µl media, 10 µl ﬁbrinogen and 3 µl thrombin, added it to a sterile
petridish and incubated for 10 minutes. Since we did not know how much of a gel it would
form we made a few drops of the mixture with the old stock, just in case poking the gel
would break some of it. Fig. 62 shows in the a) row a picture before incubation on the left
and a picture after incubation on the right. It is clear that neither of the old stock droplets
formed a gel. The b) row shows the mixture before incubation on the left and a picture after
incubation on the right. It was already clear upon mixing the new components together that
the viscosity of the solution was a lot higher and after incubation a clear gel was formed that
could be scooped up and handled.
198
==PAGE:219==
Figure 62: Geliﬁcation test from old stock and new stock. a) row showing before and after
with no geliﬁcation. b) row showing geliﬁcation from before to after.
16.9
F experiments and pipetting
From initial experiments in Fig. 47 we choose robot speed 3, as we were not aware that
cross linking was and issue. Now we are testing robot speed 1, 2 and 3, to see how the strips
behave when removing slurry by pipetting and by enzymatic degradation. This time also
added ACA to the slurry media to see if it would help keep the ﬁbrinogen network even
though we printed with this. The A row got slurry removed by pipetting and the B row got
slurry removed by enzymadic degradation. The timeline can be seen in Tab. 15

From this it is clear that the new stock has made a huge improvement. All the printed

no eﬀect. We have also proved that adding media 2 hours after printing is possible, which is
important for cell work. It is worth noting that when using a pipette to remove slurry you
can interfere with the constructs shape compared to enzymatic degradation.
